<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">This article is about the history of film as an artistic medium. </tr><tr><td class="sidebar-navbar" style="padding-top:0"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1063604349" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="navbar plainlinks hlist navbar-mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Years_in_film" title="Template:Years in film"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Years_in_film" title="Template talk:Years in film"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Years_in_film&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>The <b>history of film</b> chronicles the development of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Visual_art" title="Visual art">visual art form</a> created using <a href="/wiki/History_of_film_technology" title="History of film technology">film technologies</a> that began in the late <a href="/wiki/19th_century" title="19th century">19th century</a>.
</p><p>Although the advent of <a href="/wiki/Film" title="Film">film</a> as an artistic medium is not clearly defined, the commercial, public screening of ten of the <a href="/wiki/Auguste_and_Louis_Lumi%C3%A8re" title="Auguste and Louis Lumière">Lumière brothers</a>' short films in Paris on 28 December 1895 can be regarded as the breakthrough of projected <a href="/wiki/Cinematography" title="Cinematography">cinematographic</a> motion pictures. There had been earlier cinematographic results and screenings by others like the <a href="/wiki/Max_Skladanowsky" title="Max Skladanowsky">Skladanowsky brothers</a>, who used their self-made <a href="/wiki/Bioscop" title="Bioscop">Bioscop</a> to display the first moving picture show to a paying audience on 1 November 1895 in Berlin, but they lacked either the quality, financial backing, stamina or the luck to find the momentum that propelled the cinématographe Lumière into a worldwide success.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup>
</p><p>Soon <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Film_production_companies" title="Film production companies">film production companies</a> and studios were established all over the world. The first decade of motion picture saw film moving from a novelty to an established mass entertainment industry. The earliest films were in black and white, under a minute long, without recorded sound and consisted of a single shot from a steady camera.
</p><p>Conventions toward a general cinematic language developed over the years with <a href="/wiki/Film_editing" title="Film editing">editing</a>, camera movements and other <a href="/wiki/Cinematic_techniques" title="Cinematic techniques">cinematic techniques</a> contributing specific roles in the <a href="/wiki/Narrativity" title="Narrativity">narrative</a> of films.
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Special_effects" title="Special effects">Special effects</a> became a feature in movies since the late 1890s, popularized by <a href="/wiki/Georges_M%C3%A9li%C3%A8s" title="Georges Méliès">Georges Méliès</a>' fantasy films. Many effects were impossible or impractical to perform in theater plays and thus added more magic to the experience of movies.
</p><p>Technical improvements added length (reaching 60 minutes for a <a href="/wiki/Feature_film" title="Feature film">feature film</a> in 1906), synchronized sound recording (mainstream since the end of the 1920s), color (mainstream since the 1930s) and 3D (temporarily popular <a href='/wiki/3D_film#The_"golden_era"_(1952–1954)' title="3D film">in the early 1950s</a> and mainstream since the 2000s). Sound ended the necessity of interruptions of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Title_cards" title="Title cards">title cards</a>, revolutionized the narrative possibilities for filmmakers, and became an integral part of moviemaking.
</p><p>Popular new media, including <a href="/wiki/Television" title="Television">television</a> (mainstream since the 1950s), <a href="/wiki/Home_video" title="Home video">home video</a> (mainstream since the 1980s), and <a href="/wiki/Internet" title="Internet">internet</a> (mainstream since the 1990s) influenced the distribution and consumption of films. Film production usually responded with content to fit the new media, and with technical innovations (including <a href="/wiki/Widescreen" title="Widescreen">widescreen</a> (mainstream since the 1950s), 3D and <a href="/wiki/4D_film" title="4D film">4D film</a>) and more spectacular films to keep theatrical screenings attractive.
</p><p>Systems that were cheaper and more easily handled (including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/8mm_film" title="8mm film">8mm film</a>, <a href="/wiki/Video" title="Video">video</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Smartphone_camera" title="Smartphone camera">smartphone cameras</a>) allowed for an increasing number of people to create films of varying qualities, for any purpose (including <a href="/wiki/Home_movies" title="Home movies">home movies</a> and <a href="/wiki/Video_art" title="Video art">video art</a>). The technical quality was usually lower than that of professional movies, but improved with <a href="/wiki/Digital_video" title="Digital video">digital video</a> and affordable high quality <a href="/wiki/Digital_camera" title="Digital camera">digital cameras</a>.
</p><p>Improving over time, digital production methods became more and more popular during the 1990s, resulting in increasingly realistic <a href="/wiki/Visual_effects" title="Visual effects">visual effects</a> and popular feature-length <a href="/wiki/Computer_animation" title="Computer animation">computer animations</a>.
</p><p>Different <a href="/wiki/Film_genre" title="Film genre">film genres</a> emerged and enjoyed variable degrees of success over time, with huge differences among, for instance, <a href="/wiki/Horror_film" title="Horror film">horror films</a> (mainstream since the 1890s), <a href="/wiki/Newsreel" title="Newsreel">newsreels</a> (prevalent in U.S. cinemas between the 1910s and the late 1960s), <a href="/wiki/Musical_film" title="Musical film">musicals</a> (mainstream since the late 1920s) and <a href="/wiki/Pornographic_film" title="Pornographic film">pornographic films</a> (experiencing a <a href="/wiki/Golden_Age_of_Porn" title="Golden Age of Porn">Golden Age</a> during the 1970s).
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Precursors">Precursors</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_film&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Precursors">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Precursors_of_film" title="Precursors of film">Precursors of film</a></div>
<p>The use of film as an art form traces its origins to several earlier traditions in the arts such as (oral) <a href="/wiki/Storytelling" title="Storytelling">storytelling</a>, <a href="/wiki/Literature" title="Literature">literature</a>, <a href="/wiki/Theatre" title="Theatre">theatre</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Visual_art" title="Visual art">visual arts</a>. <a href="/wiki/Cantastoria" title="Cantastoria">Cantastoria</a> and similar ancient traditions combined storytelling with series of images that were shown or indicated one after the other. Predecessors to film that had already utilized light and shadows to create art before the advent of modern film technology include <a href="/wiki/Shadowgraphy_(performing_art)" title="Shadowgraphy (performing art)">shadowgraphy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shadow_play" title="Shadow play">shadow puppetry</a>, <a href="/wiki/Camera_obscura" title="Camera obscura">camera obscura</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Magic_lantern" title="Magic lantern">magic lantern</a>.
</p><p>Shadowgraphy and shadow puppetry represent early examples of the intent to use moving imagery for entertainment and storytelling.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:02_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-:02-2">[2]</a></sup> Thought to have originated in the <a href="/wiki/Far_East" title="Far East">Far East</a>, the art form used <a href="/wiki/Shadow" title="Shadow">shadows</a> cast by hands or objects to assist in the creation of narratives. Shadow puppetry enjoyed popularity for centuries around Asia, notably in <a href="/wiki/Java" title="Java">Java</a>, and eventually spread to Europe during the <a href="/wiki/Age_of_Enlightenment" title="Age of Enlightenment">Age of Enlightenment</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup>
</p><p>By the 16th century, entertainers often conjured images of ghostly apparitions utilizing techniques such as camera obscura and other forms of projection to enhance their performances.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> Magic lantern shows developed in the latter half of the 17th century seem to have continued this tradition with images of death, monsters and other scary figures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> Around 1790, this practice was developed into a type of multimedia ghost show known as <a href="/wiki/Phantasmagoria" title="Phantasmagoria">phantasmagoria</a>. These popular shows entertained audiences using mechanical slides, rear projection, mobile projectors, <a href="/wiki/Superimposition" title="Superimposition">superimposition</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dissolve_(filmmaking)" title="Dissolve (filmmaking)">dissolves</a>, live actors, smoke (on which projections may have been cast), odors, sounds and even electric shocks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> While many first magic lantern shows were intended to frighten viewers, advances by projectionists allowed for creative and even educational storytelling that could appeal to wider family audiences.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> Newly pioneered techniques such as the use of <a href="/wiki/Dissolving_views" title="Dissolving views">dissolving views</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Chromatrope" title="Chromatrope">chromatrope</a> allowed for smoother transitions between two projected images and aided in providing stronger narratives.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1833, scientific study of a <a href="/wiki/Stroboscopic_effect" title="Stroboscopic effect">stroboscopic</a> illusion in spoked wheels by <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Plateau" title="Joseph Plateau">Joseph Plateau</a>, <a href="/wiki/Michael_Faraday" title="Michael Faraday">Michael Faraday</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Simon_Stampfer" title="Simon Stampfer">Simon Stampfer</a> led to the invention of the Fantascope, also known as the stroboscopic disk or the <a href="/wiki/Phenakistiscope" title="Phenakistiscope">phenakistiscope</a>, which was popular in several European countries for a while. Plateau thought it could be further developed for use in phantasmagoria and Stampfer imagined a system for longer scenes with strips on rollers, as well as a transparent version (probably intended for projection). Plateau, <a href="/wiki/Charles_Wheatstone" title="Charles Wheatstone">Charles Wheatstone</a>, <a href="/wiki/Antoine_Claudet" title="Antoine Claudet">Antoine Claudet</a> and others tried to combine the technique with the <a href="/wiki/Stereoscope" title="Stereoscope">stereoscope</a> (introduced in 1838) and <a href="/wiki/Photography" title="Photography">photography</a> (introduced in 1839) for a more complete illusion of reality, but for decades such experiments were mostly hindered by the need for long exposure times, with motion blur around objects that moved while the reflected light fell on the photo-sensitive chemicals. A few people managed to get decent results from <a href="/wiki/Stop_motion" title="Stop motion">stop motion</a> techniques, but these were only very rarely marketed and no form of animated photography had much cultural impact before the advent of chronophotography.
</p>
<h3><span id="1878.E2.80.931890s:_Chronophotography.2C_animated_recordings_and_early_cinematography"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="1878–1890s:_Chronophotography,_animated_recordings_and_early_cinematography">1878–1890s: Chronophotography, animated recordings and early cinematography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_film&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: 1878–1890s: Chronophotography, animated recordings and early cinematography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The_Horse_in_motion._%22Abe_Edgington,%22_owned_by_Leland_Stanford;_driven_by_C._Marvin,_trotting_at_a_2-24_gait_over_the_Palo_Alto_track,_15th_June_1878_LOC_13624627695.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="652" data-file-width="1024" decoding="async" height="140" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/The_Horse_in_motion._%22Abe_Edgington%2C%22_owned_by_Leland_Stanford%3B_driven_by_C._Marvin%2C_trotting_at_a_2-24_gait_over_the_Palo_Alto_track%2C_15th_June_1878_LOC_13624627695.jpg/220px-thumbnail.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/The_Horse_in_motion._%22Abe_Edgington%2C%22_owned_by_Leland_Stanford%3B_driven_by_C._Marvin%2C_trotting_at_a_2-24_gait_over_the_Palo_Alto_track%2C_15th_June_1878_LOC_13624627695.jpg/330px-thumbnail.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/The_Horse_in_motion._%22Abe_Edgington%2C%22_owned_by_Leland_Stanford%3B_driven_by_C._Marvin%2C_trotting_at_a_2-24_gait_over_the_Palo_Alto_track%2C_15th_June_1878_LOC_13624627695.jpg/440px-thumbnail.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:The_Horse_in_motion._%22Abe_Edgington,%22_owned_by_Leland_Stanford;_driven_by_C._Marvin,_trotting_at_a_2-24_gait_over_the_Palo_Alto_track,_15th_June_1878_LOC_13624627695.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>"Abe Edgington," owned by Leland Stanford; driven by C. Marvin, trotting at a 2:24 gait over the Palo Alto track, 15 June 1878</i></div></div></div>
<p>Most early photographic sequences, known as <a href="/wiki/Chronophotography" title="Chronophotography">chronophotography</a>, were not initially intended to be viewed in motion and were typically presented as a serious, even scientific, method of studying locomotion. The sequences almost exclusively involved humans or animals performing a simple movement in front of the camera.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> Starting in 1878 with the publication of <i><a href="/wiki/The_Horse_in_Motion" title="The Horse in Motion">The Horse in Motion</a></i> cabinet cards, photographer <a href="/wiki/Eadweard_Muybridge" title="Eadweard Muybridge">Eadweard Muybridge</a> began making hundreds of chronophotographic studies of the motion of animals and humans in real-time. He was soon followed by other chronophotographers like <a href="/wiki/%C3%89tienne-Jules_Marey" title="Étienne-Jules Marey">Étienne-Jules Marey</a>, <a href="/wiki/Georges_Demen%C3%BF" title="Georges Demenÿ">Georges Demenÿ</a>, <a href="/wiki/Albert_Londe" title="Albert Londe">Albert Londe</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ottomar_Ansch%C3%BCtz" title="Ottomar Anschütz">Ottomar Anschütz</a>. In 1879, Muybridge started lecturing on animal locomotion and used his <a href="/wiki/Zoopraxiscope" title="Zoopraxiscope">Zoopraxiscope</a> to project animations of the contours of his recordings, traced onto glass discs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1887, the German inventor and photographer Ottomar Anschütz started presenting his chronophotographic recordings in motion, using a device he called the Elektrischen Schnellseher (also known as the <a href="/wiki/Electrotachyscope" title="Electrotachyscope">Electrotachyscope</a>), which displayed short loops on a small milk glass screen. By 1891, he had started mass production of a more economical, coin-operated peep-box viewing device of the same name that was exhibited at international exhibitions and fairs. Some machines were installed for longer periods, including some at <a href="/wiki/The_Crystal_Palace" title="The Crystal Palace">The Crystal Palace</a> in London, and in several U.S. stores. Shifting the focus of the medium from technical and scientific interest in motion to entertainment for the masses, he recorded wrestlers, dancers, acrobats, and scenes of everyday life. Though little evidence remains for most of these recordings, some scenes probably depicted staged comical scenes. Extant records suggest some of his output directly influenced later works by the Edison Company, such as the 1894 film <i><a href="/wiki/Fred_Ott%27s_Sneeze" title="Fred Ott's Sneeze">Fred Ott's Sneeze</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-copycats_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-copycats-12">[12]</a></sup>
</p><p>Advances towards motion picture projection technologies were based on the popularity of magic lanterns, chronophotographic demonstrations, and other closely related forms of projected entertainment such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Illustrated_songs" title="Illustrated songs">illustrated songs</a>. From October 1892 to March 1900, inventor <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Charles-%C3%89mile_Reynaud" title="Charles-Émile Reynaud">Émile Reynaud</a> exhibited his <a href="/wiki/Th%C3%A9%C3%A2tre_Optique" title="Théâtre Optique">Théâtre Optique</a> ("Optical Theatre") film system at the <a href="/wiki/Mus%C3%A9e_Gr%C3%A9vin" title="Musée Grévin">Musée Grévin</a> in Paris. Reynaud's device, which projected a series of animated stories such as <i><a href="/wiki/Pauvre_Pierrot" title="Pauvre Pierrot">Pauvre Pierrot</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Autour_d%27une_cabine" title="Autour d'une cabine">Autour d'une cabine</a></i>, was displayed to over 500,000 visitors over the course of 12,800 shows.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-emilereynaud.fr_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-emilereynaud.fr-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-myrent1989_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-myrent1989-14">[14]</a></sup> On 25, 29 and 30 November 1894, Ottomar Anschütz projected moving images from <a href="/wiki/Electrotachyscope" title="Electrotachyscope">Electrotachyscope</a> discs on a large screen in the darkened Grand Auditorium of a Post Office Building in Berlin. From 22 February to 30 March 1895, a commercial 1.5-hour program of 40 different scenes was screened for audiences of 300 people at the old Reichstag and received circa 4,000 visitors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kino5_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-kino5-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-before1896_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-before1896-17">[17]</a></sup>
</p><p>Illustrated songs were a trend that began in 1894 in <a href="/wiki/Vaudeville" title="Vaudeville">vaudeville</a> houses and persisted until the late 1930s in film theaters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_House_of_Witmark2_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-The_House_of_Witmark2-18">[18]</a></sup> Live performance or sound recordings were paired with hand-colored glass <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Magic_lantern_(projector)" title="Magic lantern (projector)">slides</a> projected through <a href="/wiki/Stereopticon" title="Stereopticon">stereopticons</a> and similar devices. In this way, song narrative was illustrated through a series of slides whose changes were simultaneous with the narrative development. Later, as the film industry took precedence, illustrated songs were used as filler material preceding films and during <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Film_reel" title="Film reel">reel changes</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_Sounds_of_Early_Cinema_ch._142_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-The_Sounds_of_Early_Cinema_ch._142-19">[19]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Berlin_Wintergarten_theatre" title="Berlin Wintergarten theatre">Berlin Wintergarten theater</a> hosted an early movie presentation by the <a href="/wiki/Max_Skladanowsky" title="Max Skladanowsky">Skladanowsky brothers</a> during the month of November 1895. Their picture show, which lasted approximately 15-minutes, was part of an evening program that lasted over three hours, which further included an assortment of variety acts. The Skladanowskys showed eight short films (circa 6 to 11 seconds if played at 16 fps), looped repeatedly, while a specially composed score was played especially loud to drown out the noise of the machinery.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Barber_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-Barber-20">[20]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span id="Novelty_Era_.281890s-1900s.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Novelty_Era_(1890s-1900s)">Novelty Era (1890s-1900s)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_film&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Novelty Era (1890s-1900s)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Advances_towards_projection">Advances towards projection</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_film&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Advances towards projection">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3><p>
In June 1889, American inventor <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Edison" title="Thomas Edison">Thomas Edison</a> assigned a lab assistant, <a href="/wiki/William_Kennedy_Dickson" title="William Kennedy Dickson">William Kennedy Dickson</a>, to help develop a device that could produce visuals to accompany the sounds produced from the <a href="/wiki/Phonograph" title="Phonograph">phonograph</a>. Building upon previous machines by Muybridge, Marey, Anschütz and others, Dickson and his team created the <a href="/wiki/Kinetoscope" title="Kinetoscope">Kinetoscope</a> peep-box viewer, with celluloid loops containing about half a minute of motion picture entertainment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:6_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-:6-21">[21]</a></sup> After an early preview on 20 May 1891,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> Edison introduced the machine in 1893. Many of the movies presented on the Kinetoscope showcased well-known vaudeville acts performing in <a href="/wiki/Edison%27s_Black_Maria" title="Edison's Black Maria">Edison's Black Maria</a> studio.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> The Kinetoscope quickly became a global sensation with multiple viewing parlors across major cities by 1895.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> As the initial novelty of the images wore off, the Edison Company was slow to diversify their repertoire of films and waning public interest caused business to slow by Spring 1895. To remedy declining profits, experiments, such as <i><a href="/wiki/The_Dickson_Experimental_Sound_Film" title="The Dickson Experimental Sound Film">The Dickson Experimental Sound Film</a></i>, were conducted in an attempt to achieve the device's original goal of providing visual accompaniment for sound recordings. Limitations in syncing the sound to the visuals, however, prevented widespread application.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> During that same period, inventors began advancing technologies towards <a href="/wiki/Movie_projector" title="Movie projector">film projection</a> that would eventually overtake Edison's peep-box format.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup></p><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:226px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:L%27ArroseurArros%C3%A9.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2661" data-file-width="3543" decoding="async" height="168" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/L%27ArroseurArros%C3%A9.jpg/224px-L%27ArroseurArros%C3%A9.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/L%27ArroseurArros%C3%A9.jpg/336px-L%27ArroseurArros%C3%A9.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/L%27ArroseurArros%C3%A9.jpg/448px-L%27ArroseurArros%C3%A9.jpg 2x" width="224"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:L%27ArroseurArros%C3%A9.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A frame from the Lumière brothers staged comedy film, <i><a href="/wiki/L%27Arroseur_Arros%C3%A9" title="L'Arroseur Arrosé">L'Arroseur Arrosé</a></i> (1895).</div></div></div>
<p>Multiple inventors including <a href="/wiki/Wordsworth_Donisthorpe" title="Wordsworth Donisthorpe">Wordsworth Donisthorpe</a>, <a href="/wiki/Louis_Le_Prince" title="Louis Le Prince">Louis Le Prince</a>, and <a href="/wiki/William_Friese-Greene" title="William Friese-Greene">William Friese-Greene</a> experimented with prototype motion picture projection devices in the pursuit of creating and displaying films. The scenes in these experiments were usually filmed with family, friends or passing traffic as the moving subjects. Most of these films never passed the experimental stage and their efforts garnered little public attention until after cinema had become successful.
</p><p>In the latter half of 1895, brothers <a href="/wiki/Auguste_and_Louis_Lumi%C3%A8re" title="Auguste and Louis Lumière">Auguste and Louis Lumière</a> filmed a number of short scenes with their invention, the <a href="/wiki/Cinematograph" title="Cinematograph">Cinématographe</a>. On 28 December 1895, the brothers gave their first commercial screening in Paris (though evidence exists of demonstrations of the device to small audiences as early as October 1895).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> The screening consisted of ten films and lasted roughly 20 minutes. The program consisted mainly of actuality films such as <i><a href="/wiki/Workers_Leaving_the_Lumi%C3%A8re_Factory" title="Workers Leaving the Lumière Factory">Workers Leaving the Lumière Factory</a></i> as truthful documents of the world, but the show also included the staged comedy <i><a href="/wiki/L%27Arroseur_Arros%C3%A9" title="L'Arroseur Arrosé">L'Arroseur Arrosé</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> The most advanced demonstration of film projection thus far, the Cinématographe was an instant success, bringing in an average of 2,500 to 3,000 francs daily by the end of January 1896.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> Following the first screening, the order and selection of films were changed often.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Lumière brothers' primary business interests were in selling cameras and film equipment to exhibitors, not the actual production of films. Despite this, filmmakers across the world were inspired by the potential of film as exhibitors brought their shows to new countries. This era of filmmaking, dubbed by film historian Tom Gunning as "the cinema of attractions", offered a relatively cheap and simple way of providing entertainment to the masses. Rather than focusing on stories, Gunning argues, filmmakers mainly relied on the ability to delight audiences through the "illusory power" of viewing sequences in motion, much as they did in the Kinetoscope era that preceded it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> Despite this, early experimentation with fiction filmmaking (both in actuality film and other genres) did occur. Films were mostly screened inside temporary storefront spaces, in tents of traveling exhibitors at fairs, or as "dumb" acts in vaudeville programs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> During this period, before the process of <a href="/wiki/Post-production" title="Post-production">post-production</a> was clearly defined, exhibitors were allowed to exercise their creative freedom in their presentations. To enhance the viewers' experience, some showings were accompanied by live musicians in an orchestra, a theatre organ, live <a href="/wiki/Sound_effect" title="Sound effect">sound effects</a> and commentary spoken by the showman or projectionist.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup>
</p><p>Experiments in film editing, special effects, narrative construction, and camera movement during this period by filmmakers in France, England, and the United States became influential in establishing an identity for film going forward. At both the Edison and Lumière studios, loose narratives such as the 1895 Edison film<i>, Washday Troubles,</i> established short relationship dynamics and simple storylines<i>.</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> In 1896, <a href="/wiki/La_F%C3%A9e_aux_Choux" title="La Fée aux Choux"><i>La Fée aux Choux</i> (<i>The Fairy of the Cabbages</i>)</a> was first released. Directed and edited by <a href="/wiki/Alice_Guy-Blach%C3%A9" title="Alice Guy-Blaché">Alice Guy</a>, the story is arguably the earliest narrative film in history, as well as the first film to be directed by a woman.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> That same year, the <a href="/wiki/Edison_Manufacturing_Company" title="Edison Manufacturing Company">Edison Manufacturing Company</a> released <a href="/wiki/The_Kiss_(1896_film)" title="The Kiss (1896 film)"><i>The May Irwin Kiss</i></a> in May to widespread financial success. The film, which featured the first kiss in cinematic history, led to the earliest known calls for <a href="/wiki/Film_censorship" title="Film censorship">film censorship</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Proliferation_of_actualities_and_newsreels">Proliferation of actualities and newsreels</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_film&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Proliferation of actualities and newsreels">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Actuality_film" title="Actuality film">Actuality film</a></div>
<p>In its infancy, film was rarely recognized as an art form by presenters or audiences. Regarded by the upper class as a "vulgar" and "lowbrow" form of cheap entertainment, films largely appealed to the working class and were often too short to hold any strong narrative potential.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup>  Initial advertisements promoted the technologies used to screen films rather than the films themselves. As the devices became more familiar to audiences, their potential for capturing and recreating events was exploited primarily in the form of <a href="/wiki/Newsreel" title="Newsreel">newsreels</a> and actualities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> During the creation of these films, cinematographers often drew upon <a href="/wiki/Aesthetics" title="Aesthetics">aesthetic</a> values established by past art forms such as <a href="/wiki/Framing_(visual_arts)" title="Framing (visual arts)">framing</a> and the intentional placement of the camera in the <a href="/wiki/Composition_(visual_arts)" title="Composition (visual arts)">composition</a> of their image.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> In a 1955 article for <i>The Quarterly of Film Radio and Television,</i> film producer and historian <a href="/wiki/Kenneth_Macgowan" title="Kenneth Macgowan">Kenneth Macgowan</a> asserted that the intentional staging and recreation of events for newsreels "brought storytelling to the screen".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:9_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-:9-41">[41]</a></sup>
</p><p>With the advertisement of film technologies over content, actualities initially began as a "series of views" that often contained shots of beautiful and lively places or performance acts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-:2-42">[42]</a></sup> Following the success of their 1895 screening, The Lumière brothers established a company and sent cameramen across the world to capture new subjects for presentation. After the cinematographer shot scenes, they often exhibited their recordings locally and then sent back to the company factory in Lyon to make duplicate prints for sale to whomever wanted them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> In the process of filming actualities, especially those of real events, filmmakers discovered and experimented with multiple camera techniques to accommodate for their unpredictable nature.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> Due to the short length (often only one shot) of many actualities, catalogue records indicate that production companies marketed to exhibitors by promoting multiple actualities with related subject matters that could be purchased to complement each other. Exhibitors who bought the films often presented them in a program and would provide spoken accompaniment to explain the action on screen to audiences.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_42-1"><a href="#cite_note-:2-42">[42]</a></sup>
</p><p>On May 20, 1895, the first paying audience for a motion picture gathered at Madison Square Garden to see a staged actuality that purported itself to be a boxing fight filmed by <a href="/wiki/Woodville_Latham" title="Woodville Latham">Woodville Latham</a> using a device called the <a href="/wiki/Eidoloscope" title="Eidoloscope">Eidoloscope</a>. Commissioned by Latham, the French inventor <a href="/wiki/Eugene_Augustin_Lauste" title="Eugene Augustin Lauste">Eugene Augustin Lauste</a> created the device with additional expertise from <a href="/wiki/William_Kennedy_Dickson" title="William Kennedy Dickson">William Kennedy Dickson</a> and crafted a mechanism that came to be known as the <a href="/wiki/Latham_loop" title="Latham loop">Latham loop</a>, which allowed for longer continuous runtimes and was less abrasive on the celluloid film.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup>
</p><p>In subsequent years, screenings of actualities and newsreels proved to be a profitable endeavor. In 1897, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Corbett-Fitzsimmons_Fight" title="The Corbett-Fitzsimmons Fight">The Corbett-Fitzsimmons Fight</a></i> was released. The film was a complete recording of a heavyweight world championship boxing match at <a href="/wiki/Carson_City,_Nevada" title="Carson City, Nevada">Carson City, Nevada</a>. It generated more income in <a href="/wiki/Box_office" title="Box office">box office</a> than in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Live_gate" title="Live gate">live gate</a> receipts and was the longest film produced at the time. Audiences had probably been drawn to the Corbett-Fitzsimmons film en masse because <a href="/wiki/James_J._Corbett" title="James J. Corbett">James J. Corbett</a> (a.k.a. Gentleman Jim) had become a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Matinee_idol" title="Matinee idol">matinee idol</a> since he had played a fictionalized version of himself in a stage play.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup>
</p><p>From 1910 on, regular newsreels were exhibited and soon became a popular way for finding out the news before the advent of television –  the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/British_Antarctic_Expedition,_1910%E2%80%9313" title="British Antarctic Expedition, 1910–13">British Antarctic Expedition</a> to the <a href="/wiki/South_Pole" title="South Pole">South Pole</a> was filmed for the newsreels as were the <a href="/wiki/Suffragette" title="Suffragette">suffragette</a> demonstrations that were happening at the same time. <a href="/wiki/F._Percy_Smith" title="F. Percy Smith">F. Percy Smith</a> was an early <a href="/wiki/Nature_documentary" title="Nature documentary">nature documentary</a> pioneer working for <a href="/wiki/Charles_Urban" title="Charles Urban">Charles Urban</a> and he pioneered the use of time lapse and micro cinematography in his 1910 documentary on the growth of flowers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BFIso0001_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-BFIso0001-47">[47]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WFH01_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-WFH01-48">[48]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="International_success:_Cheap_entertainment_and_development_of_early_industry">International success: Cheap entertainment and development of early industry</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_film&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: International success: Cheap entertainment and development of early industry">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span id="Early_French_Industry:_Georges_M.C3.A9li.C3.A8s.2C_Path.C3.A9_Fr.C3.A8res.2C_Gaumont_Film_Company"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_French_Industry:_Georges_Méliès,_Pathé_Frères,_Gaumont_Film_Company">Early French Industry: Georges Méliès, Pathé Frères, Gaumont Film Company</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_film&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Early French Industry: Georges Méliès, Pathé Frères, Gaumont Film Company">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:220px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Melies%27s_Montreuil_studio.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="850" data-file-width="1066" decoding="async" height="174" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Melies%27s_Montreuil_studio.jpg/218px-Melies%27s_Montreuil_studio.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Melies%27s_Montreuil_studio.jpg/327px-Melies%27s_Montreuil_studio.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Melies%27s_Montreuil_studio.jpg/436px-Melies%27s_Montreuil_studio.jpg 2x" width="218"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Melies%27s_Montreuil_studio.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Georges_M%C3%A9li%C3%A8s" title="Georges Méliès">Georges Méliès</a> (left) painting a backdrop in his studio</div></div></div>
<p>Following the successful exhibition of the Cinématographe, development of a motion picture industry rapidly accelerated in France. Multiple filmmakers experimented with the technology as they worked to attain the same success that the Lumière brothers had with their screening. These filmmakers established new companies such as the <a href="/wiki/Star_Film_Company" title="Star Film Company">Star Film Company</a>, <a href="/wiki/Path%C3%A9" title="Pathé">Pathé Frères</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Gaumont_Film_Company" title="Gaumont Film Company">Gaumont Film Company</a>.
</p><p>The most widely cited progenitor of narrative filmmaking is the French filmmaker, <a href="/wiki/Georges_M%C3%A9li%C3%A8s" title="Georges Méliès">Georges Méliès</a>. Méliès was an illusionist who had previously used magic lantern projections to enhance his magic act. In 1895, Méliès attended the demonstration of the Cinematographe and recognized the potential of the device to aid his act. He attempted to buy a device from the Lumière brothers, but they refused.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gazetas,_Aristides_2000222_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gazetas,_Aristides_2000222-49">[49]</a></sup> Months later, he bought a camera from Robert W. Paul and began experiments with the device by creating actualities. During this period of experimentation, Méliès discovered and implemented various special effects including the <a href="/wiki/Substitution_splice" title="Substitution splice">stop trick</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Multiple_exposure" title="Multiple exposure">multiple exposure</a>, and the use of <a href="/wiki/Dissolve_(filmmaking)" title="Dissolve (filmmaking)">dissolves</a> in his films.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:6_21-1"><a href="#cite_note-:6-21">[21]</a></sup>At the end of 1896, Méliès established the <a href="/wiki/Star_Film_Company" title="Star Film Company">Star Film Company</a> and started producing, directing, and distributing a body of work that would eventually contain over 500 short films.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> Recognizing the narrative potential afforded by combining his theater background with the newly discovered effects for the camera, Méliès designed an elaborate stage that contained trapdoors and a <a href="/wiki/Fly_system" title="Fly system">fly system</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:9_41-1"><a href="#cite_note-:9-41">[41]</a></sup>  The stage construction and editing techniques allowed for the development of more complex stories, such as the 1896 film, <a href="/wiki/The_House_of_the_Devil_(1896_film)" title="The House of the Devil (1896 film)"><i>Le Manoir du Diable</i> (<i>The House of the Devil</i>)</a>, regarded as a first in the <a href="/wiki/Horror_film" title="Horror film">horror film</a> genre, and the 1899 film <a href="/wiki/Cinderella_(1899_film)" title="Cinderella (1899 film)"><i>Cendrillon</i> (<i>Cinderella</i>)</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> In Méliès' films, he based the placement of the camera on the theatrical construct of <a href="/wiki/Proscenium" title="Proscenium">proscenium</a> framing, the metaphorical plane or <a href="/wiki/Fourth_wall" title="Fourth wall">fourth wall</a> that divides the actors and the audience.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> Throughout his career, Méliès consistently placed the camera in a fixed position and eventually fell out of favor with audiences as other filmmakers experimented with more complex and creative techniques.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> Méliès is most <a href="/wiki/Georges_M%C3%A9li%C3%A8s_in_culture" title="Georges Méliès in culture">widely known today</a> for his 1902 film, <i><a href="/wiki/A_Trip_to_the_Moon" title="A Trip to the Moon">Le Voyage Dans La Lune (A Trip to the Moon)</a></i>, where he utilized his expertise in effects and narrative construction to create the first <a href="/wiki/Science_fiction_film" title="Science fiction film">science fiction film</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:5_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-:5-55">[55]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1900, <a href="/wiki/Charles_Path%C3%A9" title="Charles Pathé">Charles Pathé</a> began film production under the <a href="/wiki/Path%C3%A9" title="Pathé">Pathé-Frères</a> brand, with <a href="/wiki/Ferdinand_Zecca" title="Ferdinand Zecca">Ferdinand Zecca</a> hired to lead the creative process.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> Prior to this focus on production, Pathé had become involved with the industry by exhibiting and selling what were likely counterfeit versions of the Kinetoscope in his phonograph shop. With the creative leadership of Zecca and the capability to mass produce copies of the films through a partnership with a French toolmaking company, Charles Pathé sought to make Pathé-Frères the leading film producer in the country. Within the next few years, Pathé-Frères became the largest film studio in the world, with satellite offices in major cities and an expanding selection of films available for presentation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Gaumont_Film_Company" title="Gaumont Film Company">Gaumont Film Company</a> was the main regional rival of Pathé-Frères. Founded in 1895 by <a href="/wiki/L%C3%A9on_Gaumont" title="Léon Gaumont">Léon Gaumont</a>, the firm initially sold photographic equipment and began film production in 1897, under the direction of <a href="/wiki/Alice_Guy-Blach%C3%A9" title="Alice Guy-Blaché">Alice Guy</a>, the industry's first female director.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> Her earlier films share many characteristics and themes with her contemporary competitors, such as the Lumières and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/M%C3%A9li%C3%A8s" title="Méliès">Méliès</a>. She explored dance and travel films, often combining the two, such as <i>Le Boléro</i> performed by Miss <a href="/wiki/Saharet" title="Saharet">Saharet</a> (1905) and <i>Tango</i> (1905). Many of Guy's early dance films were popular in music-hall attractions such as the <a href="/wiki/Serpentine_dance" title="Serpentine dance">serpentine dance</a> films – also a staple of the Lumières and <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Edison" title="Thomas Edison">Thomas Edison</a> film catalogs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Alice_Guy_Blaché_Cinema_Pioneer_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-Alice_Guy_Blaché_Cinema_Pioneer-59">[59]</a></sup> In 1906, she made <i>The Life of Christ</i>, a big-budget production for the time, which included 300 extras. 
</p>
<h4><span id="Contributions_in_England:_Robert_W_Paul.2C_Cecil_Hepworth.2C_The_Brighton_School"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Contributions_in_England:_Robert_W_Paul,_Cecil_Hepworth,_The_Brighton_School">Contributions in England: Robert W Paul, Cecil Hepworth, The Brighton School</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_film&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Contributions in England: Robert W Paul, Cecil Hepworth, The Brighton School">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:277px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Paul3.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="227" data-file-width="603" decoding="async" height="104" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/6/60/Paul3.gif/275px-Paul3.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/6/60/Paul3.gif/413px-Paul3.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/6/60/Paul3.gif/550px-Paul3.gif 2x" width="275"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Paul3.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The two scenes in Robert W. Paul's 1898 film, <i><a href="/wiki/Come_Along,_Do!" title="Come Along, Do!">Come Along, Do!</a></i></div></div></div>
<p>Both <a href="/wiki/Cecil_Hepworth" title="Cecil Hepworth">Cecil Hepworth</a> and <a href="/wiki/Robert_W._Paul" title="Robert W. Paul">Robert W. Paul</a> experimented with the use of different camera techniques in their films. Paul's 'Cinematograph Camera No. 1' of 1895 was the first camera to feature reverse-cranking, which allowed the same film footage to be exposed several times, thereby creating <a href="/wiki/Multiple_exposure" title="Multiple exposure">multiple exposures</a>. This technique was first used in his 1901 film <i><a href="/wiki/Scrooge,_or,_Marley%27s_Ghost" title="Scrooge, or, Marley's Ghost">Scrooge, or, Marley's Ghost</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> Both filmmakers experimented with the speeds of the camera to generate new effects. Paul shot scenes from <i>On a Runaway Motor Car through Piccadilly Circus</i> (1899) by cranking the camera apparatus very slowly.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> When the film was projected at the usual 16 frames per second, the scenery appeared to be passing at great speed. Hepworth used the opposite effect in <i>The Indian Chief and the Seidlitz Powder</i> (1901). The Chief's movements are sped up by cranking the camera much faster than 16 frames per second, producing what modern audiences would call a "<a href="/wiki/Slow_motion" title="Slow motion">slow motion</a>" effect.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup>
</p><p>The first films to move from single shots to successive scenes began around the turn of the <a href="/wiki/20th_century" title="20th century">20th century</a>. Due to the <a href="/wiki/Lost_film" title="Lost film">loss of many early films</a>, a conclusive shift from static singular shots to a series of scenes can be hard to determine. Despite these limitations, Michael Brooke of the <a href="/wiki/British_Film_Institute" title="British Film Institute">British Film Institute</a> attributes real film continuity, involving action moving from one sequence into another, to Robert W. Paul's 1898 film, <i><a href="/wiki/Come_Along,_Do!" title="Come Along, Do!">Come Along, Do!</a></i>. Only a still from the second shot remains extant today.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BFIso01_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-BFIso01-63">[63]</a></sup> Released in 1901, the British film <i><a href="/wiki/Attack_on_a_China_Mission" title="Attack on a China Mission">Attack on a China Mission</a></i> was one of the first films to show a continuity of action across multiple scenes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:9_41-2"><a href="#cite_note-:9-41">[41]</a></sup> The use of the <a href="/wiki/Intertitle" title="Intertitle">intertitle</a> to explain actions and dialogue on screen began in the early 1900s. Filmed intertitles were first used in Robert W. Paul's film, <i>Scrooge, or Marley's Ghost.</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> In most countries, <a href="/wiki/Intertitle" title="Intertitle">intertitles</a> gradually came to be used to provide dialogue and narration for the film, thus dispensing the need for narration provided by exhibitors.
</p><p>
Development of continuous action across multiple shots was furthered in England by a loosely associated group of film pioneers collectively termed "the <a href="/wiki/Brighton_School_(filmmaking)" title="Brighton School (filmmaking)">Brighton School</a>". These filmmakers included <a href="/wiki/George_Albert_Smith_(filmmaker)" title="George Albert Smith (filmmaker)">George Albert Smith</a> and <a href="/wiki/James_Williamson_(film_pioneer)" title="James Williamson (film pioneer)">James Williamson</a>, among others. Smith and Williamson experimented with action continuity and were likely the first to incorporate the use of <a href="/wiki/Insert_(filmmaking)" title="Insert (filmmaking)">inserts</a> and <a href="/wiki/Close-up" title="Close-up">close-ups</a> between shots.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:9_41-3"><a href="#cite_note-:9-41">[41]</a></sup> A basic technique for trick cinematography was the <a href="/wiki/Multiple_exposure" title="Multiple exposure">double exposure</a> of the film in the camera. The effect was pioneered by Smith in the 1898 film, <i><a href="/wiki/Photographing_a_Ghost" title="Photographing a Ghost">Photographing a Ghost</a></i>. According to Smith's catalogue records, the (now <a href="/wiki/Lost_film" title="Lost film">lost</a>) film chronicles a photographer's struggle to capture a ghost on camera. Utilizing the double exposure of the film, Smith overlaid a transparent ghostly figure onto the background in a comical manner to taunt the photographer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> Smith's <i>The Corsican Brothers</i> was described in the catalogue of the <a href="/wiki/Warwick_Trading_Company" title="Warwick Trading Company">Warwick Trading Company</a> in 1900: "By extremely careful photography the ghost appears *quite transparent*. After indicating that he has been killed by a sword-thrust, and appealing for vengeance, he disappears. A 'vision' then appears showing the fatal duel in the snow."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> Smith also initiated the special effects technique of <a href="/wiki/Reverse_motion" title="Reverse motion">reverse motion</a>. He did this by repeating the action a second time, while filming it with an inverted camera, and then joining the tail of the second negative to that of the first.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> The first films made using this device were <i>Tipsy, Topsy, Turvy</i> and <i>The Awkward Sign Painter</i>. The earliest surviving example of this technique is Smith's <i><a href="/wiki/The_House_That_Jack_Built_(1900_film)" title="The House That Jack Built (1900 film)">The House That Jack Built</a></i>, made before September 1900. Cecil Hepworth took this technique further by printing the <a href="/wiki/Negative_(photography)" title="Negative (photography)">negatives</a> of the forward motion in reverse frame by frame, producing a print in which the original action was exactly reversed. To do this he built a special printer in which the negative running through a projector was projected into the gate of a camera through a special lens giving a same-size image. This arrangement came to be called a "projection printer", and eventually an "<a href="/wiki/Optical_printer" title="Optical printer">optical printer</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> </p><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:277px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Telesc4.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="229" data-file-width="606" decoding="async" height="104" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f6/Telesc4.gif/275px-Telesc4.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f6/Telesc4.gif/413px-Telesc4.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f6/Telesc4.gif/550px-Telesc4.gif 2x" width="275"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Telesc4.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The first two shots of <i><a href="/wiki/As_Seen_Through_a_Telescope" title="As Seen Through a Telescope">As Seen Through a Telescope</a></i> (1900), with the telescope POV simulated by the circular mask</div></div></div><p>In 1898, George Albert Smith experimented with close-ups, filming shots of a man drinking beer and a woman using sniffing tobacco.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:9_41-4"><a href="#cite_note-:9-41">[41]</a></sup> The following year, Smith made <i><a href="/wiki/The_Kiss_in_the_Tunnel" title="The Kiss in the Tunnel">The Kiss in the Tunnel</a>,</i> a sequence consisting of three shots: a train enters a tunnel; a man and a woman exchange a brief kiss in the darkness and then return to their seats; the train exits the tunnel. Smith created the scenario in response to the success of a genre known as a <a href="/wiki/Phantom_ride" title="Phantom ride">phantom ride</a>. In a phantom ride film, cameras would capture the motion and surroundings from the front of a moving train.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> The separate shots, when edited together, formed a distinct sequence of events and established <a href="/wiki/Causality" title="Causality">causality</a> from one shot to the next.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> Following <i>The Kiss in the Tunnel</i>, Smith more definitively experimented with continuity of action across successive shots and began utilizing inserts in his films, such as <i><a href="/wiki/Grandma%27s_Reading_Glass" title="Grandma's Reading Glass">Grandma's Reading Glass</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Mary_Jane%27s_Mishap" title="Mary Jane's Mishap">Mary Jane's Mishap</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:9_41-5"><a href="#cite_note-:9-41">[41]</a></sup> In 1900, Smith made <i><a href="/wiki/As_Seen_Through_a_Telescope" title="As Seen Through a Telescope">As Seen Through a Telescope</a>.</i> The main shot shows a street scene with a young man tying the shoelace and then caressing the foot of his girlfriend, while an old man observes this through a telescope. There is then a cut to close shot of the hands on the girl's foot shown inside a black circular mask, and then a cut back to the continuation of the original scene.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BFIso001_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-BFIso001-72">[72]</a></sup>
</p><p>James Williamson perfected narrative building techniques in his 1900 film, <i><a href="/wiki/Attack_on_a_China_Mission" title="Attack on a China Mission">Attack on a China Mission</a></i>. The film, which film historian <a href="/wiki/John_Barnes_(film_historian)" title="John Barnes (film historian)">John Barnes</a> later described as having "the most fully developed narrative of any film made in England up to that time",  opens as the first shot shows Chinese Boxer rebels at the gate; it then cuts to the missionary family in the garden, where a fight ensues. The wife signals to British sailors from the balcony, who come and rescue them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> The film also used the first "reverse angle" cut in film history.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> The following year, Williamson created <i><a href="/wiki/The_Big_Swallow" title="The Big Swallow">The Big Swallow</a></i>. In the film. a man becomes irritated by the presence of the filmmaker and "swallows" the camera and its operator through the use of interpolated close-up shots.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> He combined these effects, along with superimpositions, use of <a href="/wiki/Film_transition" title="Film transition">wipe transitions</a> to denote a scene change, and other techniques to create a film language, or "<a href="/wiki/Film_grammar" title="Film grammar">film grammar</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup>  James Williamson's use of continuous action in his 1901 film, <i><a href="/wiki/Stop_Thief!" title="Stop Thief!">Stop Thief!</a></i> stimulated a film genre known as the "chase film."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-:1-78">[78]</a></sup> In the film, a tramp steals a leg of mutton from a butcher's boy in the first shot, is chased by the butcher's boy and assorted dogs in the following shot, and is finally caught by the dogs in the third shot.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_78-1"><a href="#cite_note-:1-78">[78]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_American_cinema:_The_Edison_Company_and_Edwin_S._Porter">Early American cinema: The Edison Company and Edwin S. Porter</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_film&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Early American cinema: The Edison Company and Edwin S. Porter">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:256px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The_Great_Train_Robbery_0018.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="576" data-file-width="720" decoding="async" height="203" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/The_Great_Train_Robbery_0018.jpg/254px-The_Great_Train_Robbery_0018.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/The_Great_Train_Robbery_0018.jpg/381px-The_Great_Train_Robbery_0018.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/The_Great_Train_Robbery_0018.jpg/508px-The_Great_Train_Robbery_0018.jpg 2x" width="254"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:The_Great_Train_Robbery_0018.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Still from <i><a href="/wiki/The_Great_Train_Robbery_(1903_film)" title="The Great Train Robbery (1903 film)">The Great Train Robbery</a></i>, produced by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Edwin_Stanton_Porter" title="Edwin Stanton Porter">Edwin S. Porter</a></div></div></div><p><i><a href="/wiki/The_Execution_of_Mary_Stuart" title="The Execution of Mary Stuart">The Execution of Mary Stuart</a></i>, produced in 1895 by the Edison Company for viewing with the <a href="/wiki/Kinetoscope" title="Kinetoscope">Kinetoscope</a>, showed <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mary_Queen_of_Scots" title="Mary Queen of Scots">Mary Queen of Scots</a> being executed in full view of the camera. The effect, known as the <a href="/wiki/Substitution_splice" title="Substitution splice">stop trick</a>, was achieved by replacing the actor with a dummy for the final shot.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup> The technique used in the film is seen as one of the earliest known uses of special effects in film.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup>
</p><p>The American filmmaker <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Edwin_Stanton_Porter" title="Edwin Stanton Porter">Edwin S. Porter</a> started making films for the <a href="/wiki/Edison_Manufacturing_Company" title="Edison Manufacturing Company">Edison Company</a> in 1901. A former projectionist hired by Thomas Edison to develop his new projection model known as the <a href="/wiki/Vitascope" title="Vitascope">Vitascope</a>, Porter was inspired in part by the works of Méliès, Smith, and Williamson and drew upon their newly crafted techniques to further the development of continuous narrative through editing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:6_21-2"><a href="#cite_note-:6-21">[21]</a></sup> When he began making longer films in 1902, he put a dissolve between every shot, just as Georges Méliès was already doing, and he frequently had the same action repeated across the dissolves.
</p><p>In 1902, Porter shot <i><a href="/wiki/Life_of_an_American_Fireman" title="Life of an American Fireman">Life of an American Fireman</a></i> for the Edison Manufacturing Company and distributed the film the following year. In the film, Porter combined <a href="/wiki/Stock_footage" title="Stock footage">stock footage</a> from previous Edison films with newly shot footage and spliced them together to convey a dramatic story of the rescue of a woman and her child by heroic firemen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:6_21-3"><a href="#cite_note-:6-21">[21]</a></sup>
</p><p>Porter's film, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Great_Train_Robbery_(1903_film)" title="The Great Train Robbery (1903 film)">The Great Train Robbery</a></i> (1903), had a running time of twelve minutes, with twenty separate shots and ten different indoor and outdoor locations. The film is seen as a first in the <a href="/wiki/Western_(genre)" title="Western (genre)">Western film genre</a> and is significant for the use of shots suggesting simultaneous action occurring at different locations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:6_21-4"><a href="#cite_note-:6-21">[21]</a></sup> Porter's use of both staged and real outdoor environments helped to create a sense of space while the placement of the camera in a wider shot established depth and allowed for an extended duration of motion on screen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup> <i>The Great Train Robbery</i> served as one of the vehicles that would launch the film medium into mass popularity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gazetas,_Aristides_2000222_49-1"><a href="#cite_note-Gazetas,_Aristides_2000222-49">[49]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup> That same year, the <a href="/wiki/Miles_Brothers" title="Miles Brothers">Miles Brothers</a> opened the first <a href="/wiki/Film_exchange" title="Film exchange">film exchange</a> in the country, which allowed permanent exhibitors to rent films from the company at a lower cost than the producers that sold their films outright.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-84">[84]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/John_P._Harris" title="John P. Harris">John P. Harris</a> opened the first permanent theater devoted exclusively to the presentation of films, the <a href="/wiki/Nickelodeon_(movie_theater)" title="Nickelodeon (movie theater)">nickelodeon</a>, in 1905 in Pittsburgh, Pennsylvania. The idea rapidly took off and by 1908, there were around 8,000 nickelodeon theaters across the country.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup> With the arrival of the nickelodeon, audience demand for a larger quantity of story films with a variety of subjects and locations led to a need to hire more creative talent and caused studios to invest in more elaborate stage designs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_84-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-84">[84]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="1910s">1910s</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_film&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: 1910s">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="New_film_producing_countries">New film producing countries</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_film&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: New film producing countries">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>

codecs="vp9, opus"'/><source data-bandwidth="200240" data-framerate="1000" data-height="240" data-shorttitle="VP9 240P" data-title="Small VP9 (240P)" data-transcodekey="240p.vp9.webm" data-width="320" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/f/f8/Cabiria_%281914%29.webm/Cabiria_%281914%29.webm.240p.vp9.webm" type='video/webm; codecs="vp9, opus"'/><source data-bandwidth="247712" data-framerate="1000" data-height="240" data-shorttitle="WebM 240P" data-title="Small WebM (240P)" data-transcodekey="240p.webm" data-width="320" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/f/f8/Cabiria_%281914%29.webm/Cabiria_%281914%29.webm.240p.webm" type='video/webm; codecs="vp8, vorbis"'/></video> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cabiria_(1914).webm" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Italian epic film <i><a href="/wiki/Cabiria" title="Cabiria">Cabiria</a></i></div></div></div>
<p>With the worldwide film boom, more countries now joined Britain, France, Germany and the United States in serious film production. In Italy, production was spread over several centres, with <a href="/wiki/Turin" title="Turin">Turin</a> being the first and biggest hub for film activity. There, <a href="/wiki/Ambrosio_Film" title="Ambrosio Film">Ambrosio</a> was the first company in the field in 1905, and remained the largest in the country through this period. Its most substantial rival was <a href="/wiki/Cines" title="Cines">Cines</a> in Rome, which started producing in 1906. The great strength of the <a href="/wiki/Cinema_of_Italy" title="Cinema of Italy">Italian industry</a> was historical epics, with large casts and massive scenery. As early as 1911, <a href="/wiki/Giovanni_Pastrone" title="Giovanni Pastrone">Giovanni Pastrone</a>'s two-reel <i>La Caduta di Troia (<a href="/wiki/The_Fall_of_Troy_(film)" title="The Fall of Troy (film)">The Fall of Troy</a>)</i> made a big impression worldwide, and it was followed by even bigger productions like <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Quo_Vadis_(1912_film)" title="Quo Vadis (1912 film)">Quo Vadis?</a></i> (1912), which ran for 90 minutes, and Pastrone's <i><a href="/wiki/Cabiria" title="Cabiria">Cabiria</a></i> of 1914, which ran for two and a half hours.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>Italian companies also had a strong line in slapstick comedy, with actors like <a href="/wiki/Andr%C3%A9_Deed" title="André Deed">André Deed</a>, known locally as "Cretinetti", and elsewhere as "Foolshead" and "Gribouille", achieving worldwide fame with his almost surrealistic gags.
</p><p>The most important film-producing country in Northern Europe up until the First World War was Denmark.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template noprint noexcerpt Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wikipedia:NOTRS" title="Wikipedia:NOTRS"><span title="This claim needs references to better sources. (August 2020)">better source needed</span></a></i>]</sup> The <a href="/wiki/Nordisk_Film" title="Nordisk Film">Nordisk</a> company was set up there in 1906 by <a href="/wiki/Ole_Olsen_(filmmaker)" title="Ole Olsen (filmmaker)">Ole Olsen</a>, a fairground showman, and after a brief period imitating the successes of French and British filmmakers, in 1907 he produced 67 films, most directed by Viggo Larsen, with sensational subjects like <i>Den hvide Slavinde (The White Slave)</i>, <i>Isbjørnejagt (Polar Bear Hunt)</i> and <i>Løvejagten (The Lion Hunt)</i>. By 1910, new smaller Danish companies began joining the business, and besides making more films about the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/White_Slavery_(prostitution)" title="White Slavery (prostitution)">white slave trade</a>, they contributed other new subjects. The most important of these finds was <a href="/wiki/Asta_Nielsen" title="Asta Nielsen">Asta Nielsen</a> in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Abyss_(1910_film)" title="The Abyss (1910 film)">Afgrunden</a> (The Abyss)</i>, directed by <a href="/wiki/Urban_Gad" title="Urban Gad">Urban Gad</a> for Kosmorama, This combined the circus, sex, jealousy and murder, all put over with great conviction, and pushed the other Danish filmmakers further in this direction. By 1912, the Danish film companies were multiplying rapidly.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Cinema_of_Sweden" title="Cinema of Sweden">Swedish film industry</a> was smaller and slower to get started than the Danish industry. Here, <a href="/wiki/Charles_Magnusson" title="Charles Magnusson">Charles Magnusson</a>, a newsreel cameraman for the <a href="/wiki/SF_Studios" title="SF Studios">Svenskabiografteatern</a> cinema chain, started fiction film production for them in 1909, directing a number of the films himself. Production increased in 1912, when the company engaged <a href="/wiki/Victor_Sj%C3%B6str%C3%B6m" title="Victor Sjöström">Victor Sjöström</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mauritz_Stiller" title="Mauritz Stiller">Mauritz Stiller</a> as directors. They started out by imitating the subjects favoured by the Danish film industry, but by 1913 they were producing their own strikingly original work, which sold very well.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>Russia began its film industry in 1908 with Pathé shooting some fiction subjects there, and then the creation of real Russian film companies by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aleksandr_Drankov" title="Aleksandr Drankov">Aleksandr Drankov</a> and <a href="/wiki/Aleksandr_Khanzhonkov" title="Aleksandr Khanzhonkov">Aleksandr Khanzhonkov</a>. The Khanzhonkov company quickly became much the largest Russian film company, and remained so until 1918.
</p><p>In Germany, <a href="/wiki/Oskar_Messter" title="Oskar Messter">Oskar Messter</a> had been involved in film-making from 1896, but did not make a significant number of films per year until 1910. When the worldwide film boom started, he, and the few other people in the German film business, continued to sell prints of their own films outright, which put them at a disadvantage. It was only when <a href="/wiki/Paul_Davidson_(producer)" title="Paul Davidson (producer)">Paul Davidson</a>, the owner of a chain of cinemas, brought Asta Nielsen and Urban Gad to Germany from Denmark in 1911, and set up a production company, Projektions-AG "Union" (<a href="/wiki/PAGU" title="PAGU">PAGU</a>), that a change-over to renting prints began. Messter replied with a series of longer films starring Henny Porten, but although these did well in the German-speaking world, they were not particularly successful internationally, unlike the Asta Nielsen films. Another of the growing German film producers just before World War I was the German branch of the French <a href="/wiki/Eclair_(company)" title="Eclair (company)">Éclair</a> company, Deutsche Éclair. This was expropriated by the German government, and turned into DECLA when the war started. But altogether, German producers only had a minor part of the German market in 1914.
</p><p>Overall, from about 1910, American films had the largest share of the market in all European countries except France, and even in France, the American films had just pushed the local production out of first place on the eve of World War I.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> While the war hindered European producers, the technical qualities of American films made them increasingly attractive to audiences everywhere.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Film_technique">Film technique</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_film&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Film technique">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:A.E._Smith_filming_The_Bargain_Fiend.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="317" data-file-width="400" decoding="async" height="174" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/A.E._Smith_filming_The_Bargain_Fiend.gif/220px-A.E._Smith_filming_The_Bargain_Fiend.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/A.E._Smith_filming_The_Bargain_Fiend.gif/330px-A.E._Smith_filming_The_Bargain_Fiend.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/b/bf/A.E._Smith_filming_The_Bargain_Fiend.gif 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:A.E._Smith_filming_The_Bargain_Fiend.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/A.E._Smith" title="A.E. Smith">A.E. Smith</a> filming <i>The Bargain Fiend</i> in the <a href="/wiki/Vitagraph_Studios" title="Vitagraph Studios">Vitagraph Studios</a> in 1907. Arc floodlights hang overhead.</div></div></div><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1097763485" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><table class="box-Unreferenced_section plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Unreferenced" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div class="mbox-image-div"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg"><img alt="" data-file-height="399" data-file-width="512" decoding="async" height="39" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" width="50"/></a></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>does not <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources">cite</a> any <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">sources</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please help <a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_film&amp;action=edit">improve this section</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Referencing_for_beginners" title="Help:Referencing for beginners">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. Unsourced material may be challenged and <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability#Burden_of_evidence" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">removed</a>.</span> <span class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">August 2020</span>)</i></span><span class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<small><a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a></small>)</i></span></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>New film techniques that were introduced in this period include the use of artificial lighting, fire effects and <a href="/wiki/Low-key_lighting" title="Low-key lighting">Low-key lighting</a> (i.e. lighting in which most of the frame is dark) for enhanced atmosphere during sinister scenes.
</p><p>Continuity of action from shot to shot was also refined, such as in Pathé's <i>le Cheval emballé (The Runaway Horse)</i> (1907) where <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cross_cutting" title="Cross cutting">cross-cutting between parallel actions</a> is used. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/David_Wark_Griffith" title="David Wark Griffith">D.
