W. Griffith</a> also began using cross-cutting in the film <i><a href="/wiki/The_Fatal_Hour_(1908_film)" title="The Fatal Hour (1908 film)">The Fatal Hour</a></i>, made in July 1908. Another development was the use of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Point_of_view_shot" title="Point of view shot">Point of View shot</a>, first used in 1910 in Vitagraph's <i>Back to Nature</i>. Insert shots were also used for artistic purposes; the Italian film <i>La mala planta (The Evil Plant)</i>, directed by <a href="/wiki/Mario_Caserini" title="Mario Caserini">Mario Caserini</a> had an insert shot of a snake slithering over the "Evil Plant".
</p><p>As films grew longer, specialist writers were employed to simplify more complex stories derived from novels or plays into a form that could be contained on one reel. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Genres" title="Genres">Genres</a> began to be used as categories; the main division was into comedy and drama, but these categories were further subdivided.
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Intertitles" title="Intertitles">Intertitles</a> containing lines of dialogue began to be used consistently from 1908 onwards, such as in Vitagraph's <i>An Auto Heroine; or, The Race for the Vitagraph Cup and How It Was Won</i>. The dialogue was eventually inserted into the middle of the scene and became commonplace by 1912. The introduction of dialogue titles transformed the nature of film narrative. When dialogue titles came to be always cut into a scene just after a character starts speaking, and then left with a cut to the character just before they finish speaking, then one had something that was effectively the equivalent of a present-day sound film.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="During_World_War_I_and_industry">During World War I and industry</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_film&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: During World War I and industry">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1097763485" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><table class="box-Unreferenced_section plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Unreferenced" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div class="mbox-image-div"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg"><img alt="" data-file-height="399" data-file-width="512" decoding="async" height="39" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" width="50"/></a></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>does not <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources">cite</a> any <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">sources</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please help <a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_film&amp;action=edit">improve this section</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Referencing_for_beginners" title="Help:Referencing for beginners">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. Unsourced material may be challenged and <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability#Burden_of_evidence" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">removed</a>.</span> <span class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">August 2020</span>)</i></span><span class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<small><a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a></small>)</i></span></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:CABINET_DES_DR_CALIGARI_01.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="886" data-file-width="1200" decoding="async" height="162" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/fd/CABINET_DES_DR_CALIGARI_01.jpg/220px-CABINET_DES_DR_CALIGARI_01.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/fd/CABINET_DES_DR_CALIGARI_01.jpg/330px-CABINET_DES_DR_CALIGARI_01.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/fd/CABINET_DES_DR_CALIGARI_01.jpg/440px-CABINET_DES_DR_CALIGARI_01.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:CABINET_DES_DR_CALIGARI_01.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The visual style of <i><a href="/wiki/The_Cabinet_of_Dr._Caligari" title="The Cabinet of Dr. Caligari">The Cabinet of Dr. Caligari</a></i> included deliberately distorted forms, complex tinting, and streaks of light painted directly onto the sets. It uses <a href="/wiki/Mise-en-sc%C3%A8ne" title="Mise-en-scène">Mise-en-scène</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>The years of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/First_World_War" title="First World War">First World War</a> were a complex transitional period for the film industry. The exhibition of films changed from short one-reel programmes to feature films. Exhibition venues became larger and began charging higher prices.
</p><p>In the United States, these changes brought destruction to many film companies, the Vitagraph company being an exception. Film production began to shift to <a href="/wiki/Los_Angeles" title="Los Angeles">Los Angeles</a> during World War I. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Universal_Studios" title="Universal Studios">Universal Film Manufacturing Company</a> was formed in 1912 as an umbrella company. New entrants included the <a href="/wiki/Jesse_L._Lasky" title="Jesse L. Lasky">Jesse Lasky</a> Feature Play Company, and Famous Players, both formed in 1913, and later amalgamated into <a href="/wiki/Famous_Players-Lasky" title="Famous Players-Lasky">Famous Players-Lasky</a>. The biggest success of these years was <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/David_Wark_Griffith" title="David Wark Griffith">David Wark Griffith</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/The_Birth_of_a_Nation" title="The Birth of a Nation">The Birth of a Nation</a></i> (1915). Griffith followed this up with the even bigger <i><a href="/wiki/Intolerance_(film)" title="Intolerance (film)">Intolerance</a></i> (1916), but, due to the high quality of film produced in the US, the market for their films was high.
</p><p>In France, film production shut down due to the general <a href="/wiki/Mobilization" title="Mobilization">military mobilization</a> of the country at the start of the war. Although film production began again in 1915, it was on a reduced scale, and the biggest companies gradually retired from production. Italian film production held up better, although so called "diva films", starring anguished female leads were a commercial failure. In Denmark, the Nordisk company increased its production so much in 1915 and 1916 that it could not sell all its films, which led to a very sharp decline in Danish production, and the end of Denmark's importance on the world film scene.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Cinema_of_Germany" title="Cinema of Germany">German film industry</a> was seriously weakened by the war. The most important of the new film producers at the time was <a href="/wiki/Joe_May" title="Joe May">Joe May</a>, who made a series of thrillers and <a href="/wiki/Adventure_film" title="Adventure film">adventure films</a> through the war years, but <a href="/wiki/Ernst_Lubitsch" title="Ernst Lubitsch">Ernst Lubitsch</a> also came into prominence with a series of very successful comedies and dramas.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="New_techniques">New techniques</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_film&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: New techniques">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Die_Austernprinzessin_screenshot.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="222" data-file-width="300" decoding="async" height="163" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Die_Austernprinzessin_screenshot.gif/220px-Die_Austernprinzessin_screenshot.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/8/8b/Die_Austernprinzessin_screenshot.gif 1.5x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Die_Austernprinzessin_screenshot.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Complex vignette shot in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Oyster_Princess" title="The Oyster Princess">die Austernprinzessin</a> (The Oyster Princess)</i></div></div></div>
<p>At this time, studios were blacked out to allow shooting to be unaffected by changing sunlight. This was replaced with floodlights and spotlights. The widespread adoption of irising-in and out to begin and end scenes caught on in this period. This is the revelation of a film shot in a circular mask, which gradually gets larger until it expands beyond the frame. Other shaped slits were used, including vertical and diagonal apertures.
</p><p>A new idea taken over from still photography was "<a href="/wiki/Soft_focus" title="Soft focus">soft focus</a>". This began in 1915, with some shots being intentionally thrown out of focus for expressive effect, as in <a href="/wiki/Mary_Pickford" title="Mary Pickford">Mary Pickford</a> starrer <i><a href="/wiki/Fanchon_the_Cricket" title="Fanchon the Cricket">Fanchon the Cricket</a></i>.
</p><p>It was during this period that camera effects intended to convey the subjective feelings of characters in a film really began to be established. These could now be done as Point of View (<a href="/wiki/Point-of-view_shot" title="Point-of-view shot">POV</a>) shots, as in Sidney Drew's <i>The Story of the Glove</i> (1915), where a wobbly hand-held shot of a door and its keyhole represents the POV of a drunken man. The use of anamorphic (in the general sense of distorted shape) images first appears in these years when <a href="/wiki/Abel_Gance" title="Abel Gance">Abel Gance</a> directed <i><a href="/wiki/La_Folie_du_Docteur_Tube" title="La Folie du Docteur Tube">la Folie du Docteur Tube</a> (The Madness of Dr. Tube)</i>. In this film the effect of a drug administered to a group of people was suggested by shooting the scenes reflected in a <a href="/wiki/Curved_mirror" title="Curved mirror">distorting mirror</a> of the fair-ground type.
</p><p>Symbolic effects taken over from conventional literary and artistic tradition continued to make some appearances in films during these years. In D. W. Griffith's <i><a href="/wiki/The_Avenging_Conscience" title="The Avenging Conscience">The Avenging Conscience</a></i> (1914), the title "The birth of the evil thought" precedes a series of three shots of the protagonist looking at a spider, and ants eating an insect. Symbolist art and literature from the turn of the century also had a more general effect on a small number of films made in Italy and Russia. The supine acceptance of death resulting from passion and forbidden longings was a major feature of this art, and states of delirium dwelt on at length were important as well.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Screenshot_from_Old_Wives_for_New.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="236" data-file-width="300" decoding="async" height="236" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/5/5b/Screenshot_from_Old_Wives_for_New.gif" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Screenshot_from_Old_Wives_for_New.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Insert shot in <i><a href="/wiki/Old_Wives_for_New" title="Old Wives for New">Old Wives for New</a></i> (<a href="/wiki/Cecil_B._DeMille" title="Cecil B. DeMille">Cecil B. DeMille</a>, 1918)</div></div></div>
<p>The use of <a href="/wiki/Insert_(filmmaking)" title="Insert (filmmaking)">insert shots</a>, i.e. close-ups of objects other than faces, had already been established by the Brighton school, but were infrequently used before 1914. It is really only with Griffith's <i>The Avenging Conscience</i> that a new phase in the use of the Insert Shot starts. As well as the symbolic inserts already mentioned, the film also made extensive use of large numbers of Big Close Up shots of clutching hands and tapping feet as a means of emphasizing those parts of the body as indicators of psychological tension.
</p><p>Atmospheric inserts were developed in Europe in the late 1910s. This kind of shot is one in a scene which neither contains any of the characters in the story, nor is a Point of View shot seen by one of them. An early example is when <a href="/wiki/Maurice_Tourneur" title="Maurice Tourneur">Maurice Tourneur</a> directed <i><a href="/wiki/The_Pride_of_the_Clan" title="The Pride of the Clan">The Pride of the Clan</a></i> (1917), in which there is a series of shots of waves beating on a rocky shore to demonstrate the harsh lives of the fishing folk. <a href="/wiki/Maurice_Elvey" title="Maurice Elvey">Maurice Elvey</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Nelson_(1918_film)" title="Nelson (1918 film)">Nelson; The Story of England's Immortal Naval Hero</a></i> (1919) has a symbolic sequence dissolving from a picture of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/William_II,_German_Emperor" title="William II, German Emperor">Kaiser Wilhelm II</a> to a peacock, and then to a battleship.
</p><p>By 1914, continuity cinema was the established mode of commercial cinema. One of the advanced continuity techniques involved an accurate and smooth transition from one shot to another. Cutting to <i>different</i> angles within a scene also became well-established as a technique for dissecting a scene into shots in American films. If the direction of the shot changes by more than ninety degrees, it is called a reverse-angle cutting. The leading figure in the full development of reverse-angle cutting was <a href="/wiki/Ralph_Ince" title="Ralph Ince">Ralph Ince</a> in his films, such as <i>The Right Girl</i> and <i>His Phantom Sweetheart</i>.
</p><p>The use of <a href="/wiki/Flashback_(narrative)" title="Flashback (narrative)">flash-back</a> structures continued to develop in this period, with the usual way of entering and leaving a flash-back being through a dissolve. The Vitagraph company's <i><a href="/wiki/The_Man_That_Might_Have_Been" title="The Man That Might Have Been">The Man That Might Have Been</a></i> (<a href="/wiki/William_J._Humphrey" title="William J. Humphrey">William J. Humphrey</a>, 1914), is even more complex, with a series of reveries and flash-backs that contrast the protagonist's real passage through life with what might have been, if his son had not died.
</p><p>After 1914, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cross_cutting" title="Cross cutting">cross cutting</a> between parallel actions came to be used –  more so in American films than in European ones. Cross-cutting was often used to get new effects of contrast, such as the cross-cut sequence in <a href="/wiki/Cecil_B._DeMille" title="Cecil B. DeMille">Cecil B. DeMille</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/The_Whispering_Chorus" title="The Whispering Chorus">The Whispering Chorus</a></i> (1918), in which a supposedly dead husband is having a liaison with a Chinese prostitute in an opium den, while simultaneously his unknowing wife is being remarried in church.
</p><p>Silent film tinting, too, gained popularity during these periods. <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Amber_tinting&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Amber tinting (page does not exist)">Amber tinting</a> meant daytime, or vividly-lit nighttime, <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Blue_tinting&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Blue tinting (page does not exist)">blue tints</a> meant dawn or dimly-lit night, red tinting represented fire scenes, green tinting meant a mysterious atmosphere, and brown tints (aka <a href="/wiki/Photographic_print_toning" title="Photographic print toning">sepia toning</a>) were used usually for full-length films instead of individual scenes. D.W. Griffiths' groundbreaking epic, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Birth_of_a_Nation" title="The Birth of a Nation">The Birth of a Nation</a></i>, the famous 1920 film <a href="/wiki/Dr._Jekyll_and_Mr._Hyde_(1920_Paramount_film)" title="Dr. Jekyll and Mr. Hyde (1920 Paramount film)"><i>Dr. Jekyll and Mr. Hyde</i></a>, and the Robert Wiene epic from the same year, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Cabinet_of_Dr._Caligari" title="The Cabinet of Dr. Caligari">The Cabinet of Dr. Caligari</a></i>, are some notable examples of tinted silent films.
</p><p><i><a href="/wiki/The_Photo-Drama_of_Creation" title="The Photo-Drama of Creation">The Photo-Drama of Creation</a></i>, first shown to audiences in 1914, was the first major screenplay to incorporate synchronized sound, moving film, and color slides.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup> Until 1927, most motion pictures were produced without sound. This period is commonly referred to as the <a href="/wiki/Silent_film" title="Silent film">silent era</a> of film.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Film_art">Film art</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_film&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Film art">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1096954695/mw-parser-output/.tmulti">.mw-parser-output .tmulti .multiimageinner{display:flex;flex-direction:column}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow{display:flex;flex-direction:row;clear:left;flex-wrap:wrap;width:100%;box-sizing:border-box}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{margin:1px;float:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .theader{clear:both;font-weight:bold;text-align:center;align-self:center;background-color:transparent;width:100%}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbcaption{background-color:transparent}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-left{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-right{text-align:right}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-center{text-align:center}@media all and (max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbinner{width:100%!important;box-sizing:border-box;max-width:none!important;align-items:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow{justify-content:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{float:none!important;max-width:100%!important;box-sizing:border-box;text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle .thumbcaption{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow>.thumbcaption{text-align:center}}</style>
<p>The general trend in the development of cinema, led from the United States, was towards using the newly developed specifically filmic devices for expression of the narrative content of film stories, and combining this with the standard dramatic structures already in use in commercial theatre. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/David_Wark_Griffith" title="David Wark Griffith">D. W. Griffith</a> had the highest standing amongst American directors in the industry, because of the dramatic excitement he conveyed to the audience through his films. <a href="/wiki/Cecil_B._DeMille" title="Cecil B. DeMille">Cecil B. DeMille</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/The_Cheat_(1915_film)" title="The Cheat (1915 film)">The Cheat</a></i> (1915), brought out the moral dilemmas facing their characters in a more subtle way than Griffith. DeMille was also in closer touch with the reality of contemporary American life. <a href="/wiki/Maurice_Tourneur" title="Maurice Tourneur">Maurice Tourneur</a> was also highly ranked for the pictorial beauties of his films, together with the subtlety of his handling of fantasy, while at the same time he was capable of getting greater <a href="/wiki/Naturalism_(literature)" title="Naturalism (literature)">naturalism</a> from his actors at appropriate moments, as in <i><a href="/wiki/A_Girl%27s_Folly" title="A Girl's Folly">A Girl's Folly</a></i> (1917).
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mr._%26_Mrs._Sidney_Drew" title="Mr. &amp; Mrs. Sidney Drew">Sidney Drew</a> was the leader in developing "polite comedy", while slapstick was refined by <a href="/wiki/Roscoe_Arbuckle" title="Roscoe Arbuckle">Fatty Arbuckle</a> and <a href="/wiki/Charlie_Chaplin" title="Charlie Chaplin">Charles Chaplin</a>, who both started with <a href="/wiki/Mack_Sennett" title="Mack Sennett">Mack Sennett</a>'s Keystone company. They reduced the usual frenetic pace of Sennett's films to give the audience a chance to appreciate the subtlety and finesse of their movement, and the cleverness of their gags. By 1917 Chaplin was also introducing more dramatic plot into his films, and mixing the comedy with sentiment.
</p><p>In Russia, <a href="/wiki/Yevgeni_Bauer" title="Yevgeni Bauer">Yevgeni Bauer</a> put a slow intensity of acting combined with Symbolist overtones onto film in a unique way.
</p><p>In Sweden, <a href="/wiki/Victor_Sj%C3%B6str%C3%B6m" title="Victor Sjöström">Victor Sjöström</a> made a series of films that combined the realities of people's lives with their surroundings in a striking manner, while Mauritz Stiller developed sophisticated comedy to a new level.
</p><p>In Germany, <a href="/wiki/Ernst_Lubitsch" title="Ernst Lubitsch">Ernst Lubitsch</a> got his inspiration from the stage work of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Max_Reinhardt_(director)" title="Max Reinhardt (director)">Max Reinhardt</a>, both in bourgeois comedy and in spectacle, and applied this to his films, culminating in his <i><a href="/wiki/The_Doll_(1919_film)" title="The Doll (1919 film)">die Puppe</a></i> (<i>The Doll</i>), <i><a href="/wiki/The_Oyster_Princess" title="The Oyster Princess">die Austernprinzessin</a></i> (<i>The Oyster Princess</i>) and <i><a href="/wiki/Madame_DuBarry_(1919_film)" title="Madame DuBarry (1919 film)">Madame DuBarry</a></i>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="1920s">1920s</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_film&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: 1920s">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span id="Golden_years_of_German_cinema.2C_Hollywood_triumphant"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Golden_years_of_German_cinema,_Hollywood_triumphant">Golden years of German cinema, Hollywood triumphant</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_film&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Golden years of German cinema, Hollywood triumphant">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Charlie_Chaplin_with_doll.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="609" data-file-width="435" decoding="async" height="308" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Charlie_Chaplin_with_doll.jpg/220px-Charlie_Chaplin_with_doll.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Charlie_Chaplin_with_doll.jpg/330px-Charlie_Chaplin_with_doll.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/e/e7/Charlie_Chaplin_with_doll.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Charlie_Chaplin_with_doll.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Charlie_Chaplin" title="Charlie Chaplin">Charlie Chaplin</a></div></div></div>
<p>At the start of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/First_World_War" title="First World War">First World War</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cinema_of_France" title="Cinema of France">French</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cinema_of_Italy" title="Cinema of Italy">Italian</a> cinema had been the most globally popular. The war came as a devastating interruption to European film industries.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Cinema_of_Germany" title="Cinema of Germany">German</a> cinema, marked by those times, saw the era of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/German_Expressionism" title="German Expressionism">German Expressionist</a> film movement. Berlin was its center with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Studio_Babelsberg" title="Studio Babelsberg">Filmstudio Babelsberg</a>, which is the oldest large-scale film studio in the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup> The first Expressionist films made up for a lack of lavish budgets by using set designs with wildly non-realistic, geometrically absurd angles, along with designs painted on walls and floors to represent lights, shadows, and objects. The plots and stories of the Expressionist films often dealt with madness, <a href="/wiki/Insanity" title="Insanity">insanity</a>, <a href="/wiki/Betrayal" title="Betrayal">betrayal</a> and other "<a href="/wiki/Intellectual" title="Intellectual">intellectual</a>" topics triggered by the experiences of World War I. Films like <i><a href="/wiki/The_Cabinet_of_Dr._Caligari" title="The Cabinet of Dr. Caligari">The Cabinet of Dr. Caligari</a></i> (1920), <i><a href="/wiki/Nosferatu" title="Nosferatu">Nosferatu</a></i> (1922) and <i><a href="/wiki/M_(1931_film)" title="M (1931 film)">M</a></i> (1931), similar to the movement they were part of, had a historic impact on film itself.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup>
Movies like <i><a href="/wiki/Metropolis_(1927_film)" title="Metropolis (1927 film)">Metropolis</a></i> (1927) and <i><a href="/wiki/Woman_in_the_Moon" title="Woman in the Moon">Woman in the Moon</a></i> (1929) partly created the genre of science fiction films.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup>
</p><p>Many German and German-based directors, actors, writers and others emigrated to the US when the <a href="/wiki/Nazism" title="Nazism">Nazis</a> gained power, giving Hollywood and the American film industry the final edge in its competition with other movie producing countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup>
</p><p>The American industry, or "Hollywood", as it was becoming known after <a href="/wiki/Hollywood,_Los_Angeles" title="Hollywood, Los Angeles">its new geographical center in California</a>, gained the position it has held, more or less, ever since: film factory for the world and exporting its product to most countries on earth.
</p><p>By the 1920s, the United States reached what is still its era of greatest-ever output, producing an average of 800 <i>feature</i> films annually,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-filmsite.org_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-filmsite.org-95">[95]</a></sup> or 82% of the global total (Eyman, 1997). The comedies of <a href="/wiki/Charlie_Chaplin" title="Charlie Chaplin">Charlie Chaplin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Buster_Keaton" title="Buster Keaton">Buster Keaton</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Swashbuckler" title="Swashbuckler">swashbuckling</a> adventures of <a href="/wiki/Douglas_Fairbanks" title="Douglas Fairbanks">Douglas Fairbanks</a> and the romances of <a href="/wiki/Clara_Bow" title="Clara Bow">Clara Bow</a>, to cite just a few examples, made these performers' faces well known on every continent. The Western visual norm that would become classical <a href="/wiki/Continuity_editing" title="Continuity editing">continuity editing</a> was developed and exported – although its adoption was slower in some non-Western countries without strong <a href="/wiki/Realism_(arts)" title="Realism (arts)">realist</a> traditions in art and drama, such as <a href="/wiki/Cinema_of_Japan" title="Cinema of Japan">Japan</a>.
</p><p>This development was contemporary with the growth of the <a href="/wiki/Studio_system" title="Studio system">studio system</a> and its greatest publicity method, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Star_system_(film)" title="Star system (film)">star system</a>, which characterized American film for decades to come and provided models for other film industries. The studios' efficient, top-down control over all stages of their product enabled a new and ever-growing level of lavish production and technical sophistication. At the same time, the system's commercial regimentation and focus on glamorous <a href="/wiki/Escapism" title="Escapism">escapism</a> discouraged daring and ambition beyond a certain degree, a prime example being the brief but still legendary directing career of the iconoclastic <a href="/wiki/Erich_von_Stroheim" title="Erich von Stroheim">Erich von Stroheim</a> in the late teens and the 1920s.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="1930s">1930s</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_film&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: 1930s">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sound_era">Sound era</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_film&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Sound era">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Sound_film" title="Sound film">Sound film</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/1930s_in_film" title="1930s in film">1930s in film</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1097763485" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><table class="box-Unreferenced_section plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Unreferenced" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div class="mbox-image-div"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg"><img alt="" data-file-height="399" data-file-width="512" decoding="async" height="39" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" width="50"/></a></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>does not <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources">cite</a> any <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">sources</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please help <a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_film&amp;action=edit">improve this section</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Referencing_for_beginners" title="Help:Referencing for beginners">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. Unsourced material may be challenged and <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability#Burden_of_evidence" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">removed</a>.</span> <span class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">August 2020</span>)</i></span><span class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<small><a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a></small>)</i></span></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:DonJuanPoster2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="960" data-file-width="677" decoding="async" height="312" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/DonJuanPoster2.jpg/220px-DonJuanPoster2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/DonJuanPoster2.jpg/330px-DonJuanPoster2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/DonJuanPoster2.jpg/440px-DonJuanPoster2.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:DonJuanPoster2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Don_Juan_(1926_film)" title="Don Juan (1926 film)">Don Juan</a> is the first feature-length film to utilize the Vitaphone sound-on-disc sound system with a synchronized musical score and sound effects, though it has no spoken dialogue.</div></div></div>
<p>During late 1927, Warners released <i><a href="/wiki/The_Jazz_Singer" title="The Jazz Singer">The Jazz Singer</a></i>, which was mostly silent but contained what is generally regarded as the first synchronized dialogue (and singing) in a feature film; but this process was actually accomplished first by <a href="/wiki/Charles_Taze_Russell" title="Charles Taze Russell">Charles Taze Russell</a> in 1914 with the lengthy film <i><a href="/wiki/The_Photo-Drama_of_Creation" title="The Photo-Drama of Creation">The Photo-Drama of Creation</a></i>. This drama consisted of picture slides and moving pictures synchronized with <a href="/wiki/Phonograph" title="Phonograph">phonograph</a> records of talks and music. The early <a href="/wiki/Sound-on-disc" title="Sound-on-disc">sound-on-disc</a> processes such as <a href="/wiki/Vitaphone" title="Vitaphone">Vitaphone</a> were soon superseded by <a href="/wiki/Sound-on-film" title="Sound-on-film">sound-on-film</a> methods like Fox <a href="/wiki/Movietone_sound_system" title="Movietone sound system">Movietone</a>, DeForest <a href="/wiki/Phonofilm" title="Phonofilm">Phonofilm</a>, and <a href="/wiki/RCA_Photophone" title="RCA Photophone">RCA Photophone</a>. The trend convinced the largely reluctant industrialists that "talking pictures", or "talkies", were the future. A lot of attempts were made before the success of <i>The Jazz Singer</i>, that can be seen in the <a href="/wiki/List_of_film_sound_systems" title="List of film sound systems">List of film sound systems</a>.
</p><p>The change was remarkably swift. By the end of 1929, Hollywood was almost all-talkie, with several competing sound systems (soon to be standardized). Total changeover was slightly slower in the rest of the world, principally for economic reasons. Cultural reasons were also a factor in countries like <a href="/wiki/Cinema_of_China" title="Cinema of China">China</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cinema_of_Japan" title="Cinema of Japan">Japan</a>, where silents co-existed successfully with sound well into the 1930s, indeed producing what would be some of the most revered classics in those countries, like <a href="/wiki/Wu_Yonggang" title="Wu Yonggang">Wu Yonggang</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/The_Goddess_(1934_film)" title="The Goddess (1934 film)">The Goddess</a></i> (China, 1934) and <a href="/wiki/Yasujir%C5%8D_Ozu" title="Yasujirō Ozu">Yasujirō Ozu</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/I_Was_Born,_But..." title="I Was Born, But...">I Was Born, But...</a></i> (Japan, 1932). But even in Japan, a figure such as the <a href="/wiki/Benshi" title="Benshi">benshi</a>, the live narrator who was a major part of Japanese silent cinema, found his acting career was ending.
</p><p>Sound further tightened the grip of major studios in numerous countries: the vast expense of the transition overwhelmed smaller competitors, while the novelty of sound lured vastly larger audiences for those producers that remained. In the case of the U.S., some historians credit sound with saving the Hollywood studio system in the face of the <a href="/wiki/Great_Depression" title="Great Depression">Great Depression</a> (Parkinson, 1995). Thus began what is now often called "The Golden Age of Hollywood", which refers roughly to the period beginning with the introduction of sound until the late 1940s. The American cinema reached its peak of efficiently manufactured glamour and global appeal during this period. The top actors of the era are now thought of as the classic film stars, such as <a href="/wiki/Clark_Gable" title="Clark Gable">Clark Gable</a>, <a href="/wiki/Katharine_Hepburn" title="Katharine Hepburn">Katharine Hepburn</a>, <a href="/wiki/Humphrey_Bogart" title="Humphrey Bogart">Humphrey Bogart</a>, <a href="/wiki/Greta_Garbo" title="Greta Garbo">Greta Garbo</a>, and the greatest box office draw of the 1930s, child performer <a href="/wiki/Shirley_Temple" title="Shirley Temple">Shirley Temple</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Creative_impact_of_sound">Creative impact of sound</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_film&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Creative impact of sound">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:WIZARD_OF_OZ_ORIGINAL_POSTER_1939.jpg"><img alt="A theatrical release poster for The Wizard of Oz (1939)." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3179" data-file-width="2094" decoding="async" height="334" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/WIZARD_OF_OZ_ORIGINAL_POSTER_1939.jpg/220px-WIZARD_OF_OZ_ORIGINAL_POSTER_1939.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/WIZARD_OF_OZ_ORIGINAL_POSTER_1939.jpg/330px-WIZARD_OF_OZ_ORIGINAL_POSTER_1939.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/WIZARD_OF_OZ_ORIGINAL_POSTER_1939.jpg/440px-WIZARD_OF_OZ_ORIGINAL_POSTER_1939.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:WIZARD_OF_OZ_ORIGINAL_POSTER_1939.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/The_Wizard_of_Oz_(1939_film)" title="The Wizard of Oz (1939 film)"><i>The Wizard of Oz</i></a></div></div></div>
<p>Creatively, however, the rapid transition was a difficult one, and in some ways, film briefly reverted to the conditions of its earliest days. The late '20s were full of static, stagey talkies as artists in front of and behind the camera struggled with the stringent limitations of the early sound equipment and their own uncertainty as to how to utilize the new medium. Many stage performers, directors and writers were introduced to cinema as producers sought personnel experienced in dialogue-based storytelling. Many major silent filmmakers and actors were unable to adjust and found their careers severely curtailed or even ended.
</p><p>This awkward period was fairly short-lived. 1929 was a watershed year: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/William_Wellman" title="William Wellman">William Wellman</a> with <i><a href="/wiki/Chinatown_Nights_(1929_film)" title="Chinatown Nights (1929 film)">Chinatown Nights</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Man_I_Love_(1929_film)" title="The Man I Love (1929 film)">The Man I Love</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/Rouben_Mamoulian" title="Rouben Mamoulian">Rouben Mamoulian</a> with <i><a href="/wiki/Applause_(1929_film)" title="Applause (1929 film)">Applause</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/Alfred_Hitchcock" title="Alfred Hitchcock">Alfred Hitchcock</a> with <i><a href="/wiki/Blackmail_(1929_film)" title="Blackmail (1929 film)">Blackmail</a></i> (Britain's first sound feature), were among the directors to bring greater fluidity to talkies and experiment with the expressive use of sound (Eyman, 1997). In this, they both benefited from, and pushed further, technical advances in microphones and cameras, and capabilities for editing and post-synchronizing sound (rather than recording all sound directly at the time of filming).
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Walt_Disney_Snow_white_1937_trailer_screenshot_(13).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="388" data-file-width="500" decoding="async" height="171" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Walt_Disney_Snow_white_1937_trailer_screenshot_%2813%29.jpg/220px-Walt_Disney_Snow_white_1937_trailer_screenshot_%2813%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Walt_Disney_Snow_white_1937_trailer_screenshot_%2813%29.jpg/330px-Walt_Disney_Snow_white_1937_trailer_screenshot_%2813%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Walt_Disney_Snow_white_1937_trailer_screenshot_%2813%29.jpg/440px-Walt_Disney_Snow_white_1937_trailer_screenshot_%2813%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Walt_Disney_Snow_white_1937_trailer_screenshot_(13).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Walt_Disney" title="Walt Disney">Walt Disney</a> introduces each of the seven dwarfs in a scene from the original 1937 <i><a href="/wiki/Snow_White_and_the_Seven_Dwarfs_(1937_film)" title="Snow White and the Seven Dwarfs (1937 film)">Snow White</a></i> theatrical trailer.</div></div></div>
<p>Sound films emphasized black history and benefited different <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Genres" title="Genres">genres</a> more so than silents did. Most obviously, the <a href="/wiki/Musical_film" title="Musical film">musical film</a> was born; the first classic-style Hollywood musical was <i><a href="/wiki/The_Broadway_Melody" title="The Broadway Melody">The Broadway Melody</a></i> (1929) and the form would find its first major creator in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Choreographer" title="Choreographer">choreographer</a>/director <a href="/wiki/Busby_Berkeley" title="Busby Berkeley">Busby Berkeley</a> (<i><a href="/wiki/42nd_Street_(film)" title="42nd Street (film)">42nd Street</a></i>, 1933, <i><a href="/wiki/Dames_(film)" title="Dames (film)">Dames</a></i>, 1934). In France, <a href="/wiki/Avant-garde" title="Avant-garde">avant-garde</a> director <a href="/wiki/Ren%C3%A9_Clair" title="René Clair">René Clair</a> made <a href="/wiki/Surrealism" title="Surrealism">surreal</a> use of song and dance in comedies like <i><a href="/wiki/Under_the_Roofs_of_Paris" title="Under the Roofs of Paris">Under the Roofs of Paris</a></i> (1930) and <i><a href="/wiki/Le_Million" title="Le Million">Le Million</a></i> (1931). <a href="/wiki/Universal_Pictures" title="Universal Pictures">Universal Pictures</a> began releasing <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gothic_horror" title="Gothic horror">gothic horror</a> films like <i><a href="/wiki/Dracula_(1931_English-language_film)" title="Dracula (1931 English-language film)">Dracula</a></i> and <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Frankenstein_(1931)" title="Frankenstein (1931)">Frankenstein</a></i> (both 1931). In 1933, <a href="/wiki/RKO_Pictures" title="RKO Pictures">RKO Pictures</a> released <a href="/wiki/Merian_C._Cooper" title="Merian C. Cooper">Merian C. Cooper</a>'s classic "giant monster" film <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/King_Kong_(1933)" title="King Kong (1933)">King Kong</a></i>. The trend thrived best in <a href="/wiki/Cinema_of_India" title="Cinema of India">India</a>, where the influence of the country's traditional song-and-dance drama made the musical the basic form of most sound films (Cook, 1990); virtually unnoticed by the Western world for decades, this Indian popular cinema would nevertheless become the world's most prolific. (<i>See also <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bollywood" title="Bollywood">Bollywood</a>.</i>)
</p><p>At this time, American <a href="/wiki/Gangster_film" title="Gangster film">gangster films</a> like <i><a href="/wiki/Little_Caesar_(film)" title="Little Caesar (film)">Little Caesar</a></i> and Wellman's <i><a href="/wiki/The_Public_Enemy" title="The Public Enemy">The Public Enemy</a></i> (both 1931) became popular. Dialogue now took precedence over "slapstick" in Hollywood comedies: the fast-paced, witty banter of <i><a href="/wiki/The_Front_Page" title="The Front Page">The Front Page</a></i> (1931) or <i><a href="/wiki/It_Happened_One_Night" title="It Happened One Night">It Happened One Night</a></i> (1934), the sexual double entrendres of <a href="/wiki/Mae_West" title="Mae West">Mae West</a> (<i><a href="/wiki/She_Done_Him_Wrong" title="She Done Him Wrong">She Done Him Wrong</a></i>, 1933) or the often subversively anarchic nonsense talk of the <a href="/wiki/Marx_Brothers" title="Marx Brothers">Marx Brothers</a> (<i><a href="/wiki/Duck_Soup_(1933_film)" title="Duck Soup (1933 film)">Duck Soup</a></i>, 1933). <a href="/wiki/Walt_Disney" title="Walt Disney">Walt Disney</a>, who had previously been in the short cartoon business, stepped into feature films with the first English-speaking animated feature <i><a href="/wiki/Snow_White_and_the_Seven_Dwarfs_(1937_film)" title="Snow White and the Seven Dwarfs (1937 film)">Snow White and the Seven Dwarfs</a></i>; released by <a href="/wiki/RKO_Pictures" title="RKO Pictures">RKO Pictures</a> in 1937. 1939, a major year for American cinema, brought such films as <i><a href="/wiki/The_Wizard_of_Oz_(1939_film)" title="The Wizard of Oz (1939 film)">The Wizard of Oz</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Gone_with_the_Wind_(film)" title="Gone with the Wind (film)">Gone with The Wind</a></i>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Color_in_cinema">Color in cinema</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_film&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Color in cinema">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Previously, it was believed that color films were first projected in 1909 at the <a href="/wiki/Palace_Theatre,_London" title="Palace Theatre, London">Palace Theatre</a> in London (the main problem with the color being that the technique, created by George Smith, (<a href="/wiki/Kinemacolor" title="Kinemacolor">Kinemacolor</a>) only used two colors: green and red, which were mixed additively). But in fact, it was in 1901 when the first color film in history was created. This untitled film was directed by photographer <a href="/wiki/Edward_Raymond_Turner" title="Edward Raymond Turner">Edward Raymond Turner</a> and his patron <a href="/wiki/Fred_Lee_(cricketer,_born_1871)" title="Fred Lee (cricketer, born 1871)">Frederick Marshall Lee</a>. The way they did it was to use black and white film rolls, but have green, red, and blue filters go over the camera individually as it shot. To complete the film, they joined the original footage and filters on a special projector. However, both the shooting of the film and its projection suffered from major unrelated issues that, eventually, sank the idea.
</p><p>Subsequently, in 1916, the <a href="/wiki/Technicolor" title="Technicolor">technicolor</a> technique arrived (trichromatic procedure (green, red, blue). Its use required a triple photographic impression, incorporation of chromatic filters and cameras of enormous dimensions. The first audiovisual piece that was completely realized with this technique was the short of <a href="/wiki/Walt_Disney" title="Walt Disney">Walt Disney</a> "<a href="/wiki/Flowers_and_Trees" title="Flowers and Trees">Flowers and Trees</a>", directed by <a href="/wiki/Burt_Gillett" title="Burt Gillett">Burt Gillett</a> in 1932. Even so, the first film to be performed with this technique will be "The Vanities Fair" (1935) by Rouben Mamoulian. Later on, the technicolor was extended mainly in the musical field as "<a href="/wiki/The_Wizard_of_Oz_(1939_film)" title="The Wizard of Oz (1939 film)">The Wizard of Oz</a>" or "<a href="/wiki/Singin%27_in_the_Rain" title="Singin' in the Rain">Singin' in the Rain</a>", in films such as "<a href="/wiki/The_Adventures_of_Robin_Hood" title="The Adventures of Robin Hood">The Adventures of Robin Hood</a>" or the animation film, "<a href="/wiki/Snow_White_and_the_Seven_Dwarfs_(1937_film)" title="Snow White and the Seven Dwarfs (1937 film)">Snow White and the Seven Dwarfs</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-esquire1_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-esquire1-96">[96]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="1940s">1940s</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_film&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: 1940s">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="World_War_II_and_its_aftermath">World War II and its aftermath</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_film&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: World War II and its aftermath">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/1940s_in_film" title="1940s in film">1940s in film</a></div>
<p>The desire for wartime propaganda against the opposition created a renaissance in the film industry in Britain, with realistic war dramas like <i><a href="/wiki/49th_Parallel_(film)" title="49th Parallel (film)">49th Parallel</a></i> (1941), <i><a href="/wiki/Went_the_Day_Well%3F" title="Went the Day Well?">Went the Day Well?</a></i> (1942), <i><a href="/wiki/The_Way_Ahead" title="The Way Ahead">The Way Ahead</a></i> (1944) and <a href="/wiki/No%C3%ABl_Coward" title="Noël Coward">Noël Coward</a> and <a href="/wiki/David_Lean" title="David Lean">David Lean</a>'s celebrated naval film <i><a href="/wiki/In_Which_We_Serve" title="In Which We Serve">In Which We Serve</a></i> in 1942, which won a special <a href="/wiki/Academy_Awards" title="Academy Awards">Academy Award</a>. These existed alongside more flamboyant films like <a href="/wiki/Michael_Powell" title="Michael Powell">Michael Powell</a> and <a href="/wiki/Emeric_Pressburger" title="Emeric Pressburger">Emeric Pressburger</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/The_Life_and_Death_of_Colonel_Blimp" title="The Life and Death of Colonel Blimp">The Life and Death of Colonel Blimp</a></i> (1943), <i><a href="/wiki/A_Canterbury_Tale" title="A Canterbury Tale">A Canterbury Tale</a></i> (1944) and <i><a href="/wiki/A_Matter_of_Life_and_Death_(film)" title="A Matter of Life and Death (film)">A Matter of Life and Death</a></i> (1946), as well as <a href="/wiki/Laurence_Olivier" title="Laurence Olivier">Laurence Olivier</a>'s <a href="/wiki/1944_in_film" title="1944 in film">1944 film</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Henry_V_(1944_film)" title="Henry V (1944 film)">Henry V</a></i>, based on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shakespearean_histories" title="Shakespearean histories">Shakespearean history</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Henry_V_(play)" title="Henry V (play)">Henry V</a></i>. The success of <i>Snow White and the Seven Dwarfs</i> allowed Disney to make more animated features like <i><a href="/wiki/Pinocchio_(1940_film)" title="Pinocchio (1940 film)">Pinocchio</a></i> (1940), <i><a href="/wiki/Fantasia_(1940_film)" title="Fantasia (1940 film)">Fantasia</a></i> (1940), <i><a href="/wiki/Dumbo" title="Dumbo">Dumbo</a></i> (1941) and <i><a href="/wiki/Bambi" title="Bambi">Bambi</a></i> (1942).
</p><p>The onset of US involvement in World War II also brought a proliferation of films as both <a href="/wiki/Patriotism" title="Patriotism">patriotism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Propaganda" title="Propaganda">propaganda</a>. American propaganda films included <i><a href="/wiki/Desperate_Journey" title="Desperate Journey">Desperate Journey</a></i> (1942), <i><a href="/wiki/Mrs._Miniver" title="Mrs. Miniver">Mrs. Miniver</a></i>  (1942), <i><a href="/wiki/Forever_and_a_Day_(1943_film)" title="Forever and a Day (1943 film)">Forever and a Day</a></i> (1943) and <i><a href="/wiki/Objective,_Burma!" title="Objective, Burma!">Objective, Burma!</a></i> (1945). Notable American films from the war years include the anti-Nazi <i><a href="/wiki/Watch_on_the_Rhine" title="Watch on the Rhine">Watch on the Rhine</a></i> (1943), scripted by <a href="/wiki/Dashiell_Hammett" title="Dashiell Hammett">Dashiell Hammett</a>; <i><a href="/wiki/Shadow_of_a_Doubt" title="Shadow of a Doubt">Shadow of a Doubt</a></i> (1943), Hitchcock's direction of a script by <a href="/wiki/Thornton_Wilder" title="Thornton Wilder">Thornton Wilder</a>; the <a href="/wiki/George_M._Cohan" title="George M. Cohan">George M. Cohan</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Biopic" title="Biopic">biopic</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/Yankee_Doodle_Dandy" title="Yankee Doodle Dandy">Yankee Doodle Dandy</a></i> (1942), starring <a href="/wiki/James_Cagney" title="James Cagney">James Cagney</a>, and the immensely popular <i><a href="/wiki/Casablanca_(film)" title="Casablanca (film)">Casablanca</a></i>, with <a href="/wiki/Humphrey_Bogart" title="Humphrey Bogart">Humphrey Bogart</a>. Bogart would star in 36 films between 1934 and 1942 including <a href="/wiki/John_Huston" title="John Huston">John Huston</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/The_Maltese_Falcon_(1941_film)" title="The Maltese Falcon (1941 film)">The Maltese Falcon</a></i> (1941), one of the first films now considered a classic <a href="/wiki/Film_noir" title="Film noir">film noir</a>. In 1941, <a href="/wiki/RKO_Pictures" title="RKO Pictures">RKO Pictures</a> released <i><a href="/wiki/Citizen_Kane" title="Citizen Kane">Citizen Kane</a></i> made by <a href="/wiki/Orson_Welles" title="Orson Welles">Orson Welles</a>. It is often considered the greatest film of all time. It would set the stage for the modern motion picture, as it revolutionized film story telling.
</p><p>The strictures of wartime also brought an interest in more fantastical subjects. These included Britain's <a href="/wiki/Gainsborough_Pictures" title="Gainsborough Pictures">Gainsborough</a> melodramas (including <i><a href="/wiki/The_Man_in_Grey" title="The Man in Grey">The Man in Grey</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Wicked_Lady" title="The Wicked Lady">The Wicked Lady</a></i>), and films like <i><a href="/wiki/Here_Comes_Mr._Jordan" title="Here Comes Mr. Jordan">Here Comes Mr. Jordan</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Heaven_Can_Wait_(1943_film)" title="Heaven Can Wait (1943 film)">Heaven Can Wait</a></i>, <i>I Married a Witch</i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Blithe_Spirit_(1945_film)" title="Blithe Spirit (1945 film)">Blithe Spirit</a></i>. <a href="/wiki/Val_Lewton" title="Val Lewton">Val Lewton</a> also produced a series of atmospheric and influential small-budget <a href="/wiki/Horror_film" title="Horror film">horror</a> films, some of the more famous examples being <i><a href="/wiki/Cat_People_(1942_film)" title="Cat People (1942 film)">Cat People</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Isle_of_the_Dead_(film)" title="Isle of the Dead (film)">Isle of the Dead</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Body_Snatcher_(film)" title="The Body Snatcher (film)">The Body Snatcher</a></i>. The decade probably also saw the so-called "women's pictures", such as <i><a href="/wiki/Now,_Voyager" title="Now, Voyager">Now, Voyager</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Random_Harvest_(film)" title="Random Harvest (film)">Random Harvest</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Mildred_Pierce_(film)" title="Mildred Pierce (film)">Mildred Pierce</a></i> at the peak of their popularity.
</p><p>1946 saw RKO Radio releasing <i><a href="/wiki/It%27s_a_Wonderful_Life" title="It's a Wonderful Life">It's a Wonderful Life</a></i> directed by Italian-born filmmaker <a href="/wiki/Frank_Capra" title="Frank Capra">Frank Capra</a>. Soldiers returning from the war would provide the inspiration for films like <i><a href="/wiki/The_Best_Years_of_Our_Lives" title="The Best Years of Our Lives">The Best Years of Our Lives</a></i>, and many of those in the film industry had served in some capacity during the war. <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Fuller" title="Samuel Fuller">Samuel Fuller</a>'s experiences in World War II would influence his largely autobiographical films of later decades such as <i><a href="/wiki/The_Big_Red_One" title="The Big Red One">The Big Red One</a></i>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Actors_Studio" title="The Actors Studio">The Actors Studio</a> was founded in October 1947 by <a href="/wiki/Elia_Kazan" title="Elia Kazan">Elia Kazan</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Robert_Lewis_(actor)" title="Robert Lewis (actor)">Robert Lewis</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Cheryl_Crawford" title="Cheryl Crawford">Cheryl Crawford</a>, and the same year <a href="/wiki/Oskar_Fischinger" title="Oskar Fischinger">Oskar Fischinger</a> filmed <i><a href="/wiki/Motion_Painting_No._1" title="Motion Painting No. 1">Motion Painting No. 1</a></i>.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:LadriDiBicicletteStaiola1948.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="720" data-file-width="960" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/LadriDiBicicletteStaiola1948.jpg/220px-LadriDiBicicletteStaiola1948.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/LadriDiBicicletteStaiola1948.jpg/330px-LadriDiBicicletteStaiola1948.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/LadriDiBicicletteStaiola1948.jpg/440px-LadriDiBicicletteStaiola1948.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:LadriDiBicicletteStaiola1948.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Italian_neorealism" title="Italian neorealism">Italian neorealist</a> movie <i><a href="/wiki/Bicycle_Thieves" title="Bicycle Thieves">Bicycle Thieves</a></i> (1948) by <a href="/wiki/Vittorio_De_Sica" title="Vittorio De Sica">Vittorio De Sica</a>, considered part of the canon of classic cinema.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>In 1943, <i><a href="/wiki/Ossessione" title="Ossessione">Ossessione</a></i> was screened in Italy, marking the beginning of <a href="/wiki/Italian_neorealism" title="Italian neorealism">Italian neorealism</a>. Major films of this type during the 1940s included <i><a href="/wiki/Bicycle_Thieves" title="Bicycle Thieves">Bicycle Thieves</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Rome,_Open_City" title="Rome, Open City">Rome, Open City</a></i>, and <i><a href="/wiki/La_Terra_Trema" title="La Terra Trema">La Terra Trema</a></i>. In 1952 <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Umberto_D" title="Umberto D">Umberto D</a></i> was released, usually considered the last film of this type.
</p><p>In the late 1940s, in Britain, <a href="/wiki/Ealing_Studios" title="Ealing Studios">Ealing Studios</a> embarked on their series of celebrated comedies, including <i><a href="/wiki/Whisky_Galore!_(1949_film)" title="Whisky Galore! (1949 film)">Whisky Galore!</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Passport_to_Pimlico" title="Passport to Pimlico">Passport to Pimlico</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Kind_Hearts_and_Coronets" title="Kind Hearts and Coronets">Kind Hearts and Coronets</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Man_in_the_White_Suit" title="The Man in the White Suit">The Man in the White Suit</a></i>, and <a href="/wiki/Carol_Reed" title="Carol Reed">Carol Reed</a> directed his influential thrillers <i><a href="/wiki/Odd_Man_Out" title="Odd Man Out">Odd Man Out</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Fallen_Idol_(film)" title="The Fallen Idol (film)">The Fallen Idol</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Third_Man" title="The Third Man">The Third Man</a></i>. <a href="/wiki/David_Lean" title="David Lean">David Lean</a> was also rapidly becoming a force in world cinema with <i><a href="/wiki/Brief_Encounter" title="Brief Encounter">Brief Encounter</a></i> and his <a href="/wiki/Charles_Dickens" title="Charles Dickens">Dickens</a> adaptations <i><a href="/wiki/Great_Expectations_(1946_film)" title="Great Expectations (1946 film)">Great Expectations</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Oliver_Twist_(1948_film)" title="Oliver Twist (1948 film)">Oliver Twist</a></i>, and <a href="/wiki/Michael_Powell" title="Michael Powell">Michael Powell</a> and <a href="/wiki/Emeric_Pressburger" title="Emeric Pressburger">Emeric Pressburger</a> would experience the best of their creative partnership with films like <i><a href="/wiki/Black_Narcissus" title="Black Narcissus">Black Narcissus</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Red_Shoes_(1948_film)" title="The Red Shoes (1948 film)">The Red Shoes</a></i>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="1950s">1950s</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_film&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: 1950s">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/1950s_in_film" title="1950s in film">1950s in film</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Charlton_Heston_as_Antony_in_Julius_Caesar,_B%26W_image_by_Chalmers_Butterfield.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2000" data-file-width="2840" decoding="async" height="155" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Charlton_Heston_as_Antony_in_Julius_Caesar%2C_B%26W_image_by_Chalmers_Butterfield.jpg/220px-Charlton_Heston_as_Antony_in_Julius_Caesar%2C_B%26W_image_by_Chalmers_Butterfield.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Charlton_Heston_as_Antony_in_Julius_Caesar%2C_B%26W_image_by_Chalmers_Butterfield.jpg/330px-Charlton_Heston_as_Antony_in_Julius_Caesar%2C_B%26W_image_by_Chalmers_Butterfield.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Charlton_Heston_as_Antony_in_Julius_Caesar%2C_B%26W_image_by_Chalmers_Butterfield.jpg/440px-Charlton_Heston_as_Antony_in_Julius_Caesar%2C_B%26W_image_by_Chalmers_Butterfield.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Charlton_Heston_as_Antony_in_Julius_Caesar,_B%26W_image_by_Chalmers_Butterfield.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A production scene from the 1950 Hollywood film <i><a href="/wiki/Julius_Caesar_(1950_film)" title="Julius Caesar (1950 film)">Julius Caesar</a></i> starring <a href="/wiki/Charlton_Heston" title="Charlton Heston">Charlton Heston</a></div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/House_Un-American_Activities_Committee" title="House Un-American Activities Committee">House Un-American Activities Committee</a> investigated Hollywood in the early 1950s. Protested by the <a href="/wiki/Hollywood_blacklist" title="Hollywood blacklist">Hollywood Ten</a> before the committee, the hearings resulted in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Blacklist" title="Blacklist">blacklisting</a> of many actors, writers and directors, including Chayefsky, <a href="/wiki/Charlie_Chaplin" title="Charlie Chaplin">Charlie Chaplin</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Dalton_Trumbo" title="Dalton Trumbo">Dalton Trumbo</a>, and many of these fled to Europe, especially the United Kingdom.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Cold_War" title="Cold War">Cold War era</a> <a href="/wiki/Zeitgeist" title="Zeitgeist">zeitgeist</a> translated into a type of near-<a href="/wiki/Paranoia" title="Paranoia">paranoia</a> manifested in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Theme_(literature)" title="Theme (literature)">themes</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Alien_invasion" title="Alien invasion">invading armies of evil aliens</a> (<i><a href="/wiki/Invasion_of_the_Body_Snatchers" title="Invasion of the Body Snatchers">Invasion of the Body Snatchers</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/The_War_of_the_Worlds_(1953_film)" title="The War of the Worlds (1953 film)">The War of the Worlds</a></i>) and <a href="/wiki/Communism" title="Communism">communist</a> <a href="/wiki/Fifth_column" title="Fifth column">fifth columnists</a> (<i><a href="/wiki/The_Manchurian_Candidate_(1962_film)" title="The Manchurian Candidate (1962 film)">The Manchurian Candidate</a></i>).
</p><p>During the immediate post-war years the cinematic industry was also threatened by television, and the increasing popularity of the medium meant that some film theatres would bankrupt and close. The demise of the "studio system" spurred the <a href="/wiki/Criticism" title="Criticism">self-commentary</a> of films like <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sunset_Boulevard_(1950_film)" title="Sunset Boulevard (1950 film)">Sunset Boulevard</a></i> (1950) and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Bad_and_the_Beautiful" title="The Bad and the Beautiful">The Bad and the Beautiful</a></i> (1952).
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:170px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:UyoonSahranah.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="400" data-file-width="280" decoding="async" height="240" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/UyoonSahranah.jpg/168px-UyoonSahranah.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/UyoonSahranah.jpg/252px-UyoonSahranah.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/7/7c/UyoonSahranah.jpg 2x" width="168"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:UyoonSahranah.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Poster for the 1956 Egyptian film <i><a href="/wiki/Wakeful_Eyes" title="Wakeful Eyes">Wakeful Eyes</a></i> starring <a href="/wiki/Salah_Zulfikar" title="Salah Zulfikar">Salah Zulfikar</a></div></div></div>
<p>In 1950, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lettrists" title="Lettrists">Lettrists</a> avante-gardists caused riots at the <a href="/wiki/Cannes_Film_Festival" title="Cannes Film Festival">Cannes Film Festival</a>, when <a href="/wiki/Isidore_Isou" title="Isidore Isou">Isidore Isou</a>'s <i>Treatise on Slime and Eternity</i> was screened. After their criticism of <a href="/wiki/Charlie_Chaplin" title="Charlie Chaplin">Charlie Chaplin</a> and split with the movement, the <a href="/wiki/Ultra-Lettrist" title="Ultra-Lettrist">Ultra-Lettrists</a> continued to cause disruptions when they showed their new <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hypergraphics" title="Hypergraphics">hypergraphical</a> techniques.
The most notorious film is <a href="/wiki/Guy_Debord" title="Guy Debord">Guy Debord</a>'s <i>Howls for Sade</i> of 1952.
Distressed by the increasing number of closed theatres, studios and companies would find new and innovative ways to bring audiences back. These included attempts to widen their appeal with new screen formats. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cinemascope" title="Cinemascope">Cinemascope</a>, which would remain a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/20th_Century_Fox" title="20th Century Fox">20th Century Fox</a> distinction until 1967, was announced with 1953's <i><a href="/wiki/The_Robe_(film)" title="The Robe (film)">The Robe</a></i>. <a href="/wiki/VistaVision" title="VistaVision">VistaVision</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cinerama" title="Cinerama">Cinerama</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Todd-AO" title="Todd-AO">Todd-AO</a> boasted a "bigger is better" approach to <a href="/wiki/Marketing" title="Marketing">marketing</a> films to a dwindling US audience. This resulted in the revival of epic films to take advantage of the new big screen formats. Some of the most successful examples of these <a href="/wiki/Bible" title="Bible">Biblical</a> and <a href="/wiki/History" title="History">historical</a> spectaculars include <i><a href="/wiki/The_Ten_Commandments_(1956_film)" title="The Ten Commandments (1956 film)">The Ten Commandments</a></i> (1956), <i><a href="/wiki/The_Vikings_(film)" title="The Vikings (film)">The Vikings</a></i> (1958), <i><a href="/wiki/Ben-Hur_(1959_film)" title="Ben-Hur (1959 film)">Ben-Hur</a></i> (1959), <i><a href="/wiki/Spartacus_(film)" title="Spartacus (film)">Spartacus</a></i> (1960) and <i><a href="/wiki/El_Cid_(film)" title="El Cid (film)">El Cid</a></i> (1961). Also during this period a number of other significant films were produced in <a href="/wiki/Todd-AO" title="Todd-AO">Todd-AO</a>, developed by <a href="/wiki/Mike_Todd" title="Mike Todd">Mike Todd</a> shortly before his death, including <i><a href="/wiki/Oklahoma!_(1955_film)" title="Oklahoma! (1955 film)">Oklahoma!</a></i> (1955), <a href="/wiki/Around_the_World_in_80_Days_(1956_film)" title="Around the World in 80 Days (1956 film)"><i>Around the World in 80 Days</i></a> (1956), <a href="/wiki/South_Pacific_(1958_film)" title="South Pacific (1958 film)"><i>South Pacific</i></a> (1958) and <i><a href="/wiki/Cleopatra_(1963_film)" title="Cleopatra (1963 film)">Cleopatra</a></i> (1963) plus many more.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Gimmick" title="Gimmick">Gimmicks</a> also proliferated to lure in audiences. The fad for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/3-D_film" title="3-D film">3-D film</a> would last for only two years, 1952–1954, and helped sell <i><a href="/wiki/House_of_Wax_(1953_film)" title="House of Wax (1953 film)">House of Wax</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Creature_from_the_Black_Lagoon" title="Creature from the Black Lagoon">Creature from the Black Lagoon</a></i>. Producer <a href="/wiki/William_Castle" title="William Castle">William Castle</a> would tout films featuring "Emergo" "Percepto", the first of a series of gimmicks that would remain popular marketing tools for Castle and others throughout the 1960s.
</p><p>In the U.S., a post-WW2 tendency toward questioning the establishment and societal norms and the early <a href="/wiki/Activism" title="Activism">activism</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Civil_rights_movement" title="Civil rights movement">civil rights movement</a> was reflected in Hollywood films such as <i><a href="/wiki/Blackboard_Jungle" title="Blackboard Jungle">Blackboard Jungle</a></i> (1955), <i><a href="/wiki/On_the_Waterfront" title="On the Waterfront">On the Waterfront</a></i> (1954), <a href="/wiki/Paddy_Chayefsky" title="Paddy Chayefsky">Paddy Chayefsky</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Marty_(film)" title="Marty (film)">Marty</a></i> and <a href="/wiki/Reginald_Rose" title="Reginald Rose">Reginald Rose</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/12_Angry_Men_(1957_film)" title="12 Angry Men (1957 film)">12 Angry Men</a></i> (1957). Disney continued making animated films, notably; <i><a href="/wiki/Cinderella_(1950_film)" title="Cinderella (1950 film)">Cinderella</a></i> (1950), <i><a href="/wiki/Peter_Pan_(1953_film)" title="Peter Pan (1953 film)">Peter Pan</a></i> (1953), <i><a href="/wiki/Lady_and_the_Tramp" title="Lady and the Tramp">Lady and the Tramp</a></i> (1955), and <i><a href="/wiki/Sleeping_Beauty_(1959_film)" title="Sleeping Beauty (1959 film)">Sleeping Beauty</a></i> (1959). He began, however, getting more involved in live action films, producing classics like <i><a href="/wiki/20,000_Leagues_Under_the_Sea_(1954_film)" title="20,000 Leagues Under the Sea (1954 film)">20,000 Leagues Under the Sea</a></i> (1954), and <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Old_Yeller_(1957_film)" title="Old Yeller (1957 film)">Old Yeller</a></i> (1957). Television began competing seriously with films projected in theatres, but surprisingly it promoted more filmgoing rather than curtailing it.
</p><p><i><a href="/wiki/Limelight_(1952_film)" title="Limelight (1952 film)">Limelight</a></i> is probably a unique film in at least one interesting respect. Its two leads, <a href="/wiki/Charlie_Chaplin" title="Charlie Chaplin">Charlie Chaplin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Claire_Bloom" title="Claire Bloom">Claire Bloom</a>, were in the industry in no less than three different centuries. In the 19th century, Chaplin made his theatrical debut at the age of eight, in 1897, in a clog dancing troupe, The Eight Lancaster Lads. In the 21st century, Bloom is still enjoying a full and productive career, having appeared in dozens of films and television series produced up to and including 2019. She received particular acclaim for her role in <i><a href="/wiki/The_King%27s_Speech" title="The King's Speech">The King's Speech</a></i> (2010).
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Golden_age_of_Asian_cinema">Golden age of Asian cinema</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_film&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Golden age of Asian cinema">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Asian_cinema" title="Asian cinema">Asian cinema</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Satyajit_Ray.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1040" data-file-width="800" decoding="async" height="286" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Satyajit_Ray.jpg/220px-Satyajit_Ray.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Satyajit_Ray.jpg/330px-Satyajit_Ray.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Satyajit_Ray.jpg/440px-Satyajit_Ray.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Satyajit_Ray.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Satyajit_Ray" title="Satyajit Ray">Satyajit Ray</a>, <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">Indian</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bengali_people" title="Bengali people">Bengali</a> film director</div></div></div>
<p>Following the end of World War II in the 1940s, the following decade, the 1950s, marked a 'golden age' for non-English <a href="/wiki/World_cinema" title="World cinema">world cinema</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Daniels_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-Daniels-99">[99]</a></sup> especially for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Asian_cinema" title="Asian cinema">Asian cinema</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lee_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lee-100">[100]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Totaro_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-Totaro-101">[101]</a></sup> Many of the most critically acclaimed Asian films of all time were produced during this decade, including <a href="/wiki/Yasujir%C5%8D_Ozu" title="Yasujirō Ozu">Yasujirō Ozu</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Tokyo_Story" title="Tokyo Story">Tokyo Story</a></i> (1953), <a href="/wiki/Satyajit_Ray" title="Satyajit Ray">Satyajit Ray</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/The_Apu_Trilogy" title="The Apu Trilogy">The Apu Trilogy</a></i> (1955–1959) and <i><a href="/wiki/Jalsaghar" title="Jalsaghar">Jalsaghar</a></i> (1958), <a href="/wiki/Kenji_Mizoguchi" title="Kenji Mizoguchi">Kenji Mizoguchi</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Ugetsu" title="Ugetsu">Ugetsu</a></i> (1954) and <i><a href="/wiki/Sansho_the_Bailiff" title="Sansho the Bailiff">Sansho the Bailiff</a></i> (1954), <a href="/wiki/Raj_Kapoor" title="Raj Kapoor">Raj Kapoor</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Awaara" title="Awaara">Awaara</a></i> (1951), <a href="/wiki/Mikio_Naruse" title="Mikio Naruse">Mikio Naruse</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Floating_Clouds" title="Floating Clouds">Floating Clouds</a></i> (1955), <a href="/wiki/Guru_Dutt" title="Guru Dutt">Guru Dutt</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Pyaasa" title="Pyaasa">Pyaasa</a></i> (1957) and <i><a href="/wiki/Kaagaz_Ke_Phool" title="Kaagaz Ke Phool">Kaagaz Ke Phool</a></i> (1959), and the <a href="/wiki/Akira_Kurosawa" title="Akira Kurosawa">Akira Kurosawa</a> films <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rashomon_(film)" title="Rashomon (film)">Rashomon</a></i> (1950), <i><a href="/wiki/Ikiru" title="Ikiru">Ikiru</a></i> (1952), <i><a href="/wiki/Seven_Samurai" title="Seven Samurai">Seven Samurai</a></i> (1954) and <i><a href="/wiki/Throne_of_Blood" title="Throne of Blood">Throne of Blood</a></i> (1957).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lee_100-1"><a href="#cite_note-Lee-100">[100]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Totaro_101-1"><a href="#cite_note-Totaro-101">[101]</a></sup>
</p><p>During <a href="/wiki/Cinema_of_Japan" title="Cinema of Japan">Japanese cinema</a>'s 'Golden Age' of the 1950s, successful films included <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rashomon_(film)" title="Rashomon (film)">Rashomon</a></i> (1950), <i><a href="/wiki/Seven_Samurai" title="Seven Samurai">Seven Samurai</a></i> (1954) and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Hidden_Fortress" title="The Hidden Fortress">The Hidden Fortress</a></i> (1958) by <a href="/wiki/Akira_Kurosawa" title="Akira Kurosawa">Akira Kurosawa</a>, as well as <a href="/wiki/Yasujir%C5%8D_Ozu" title="Yasujirō Ozu">Yasujirō Ozu</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Tokyo_Story" title="Tokyo Story">Tokyo Story</a></i> (1953) and <a href="/wiki/Ishir%C5%8D_Honda" title="Ishirō Honda">Ishirō Honda</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Godzilla_(1954_film)" title="Godzilla (1954 film)">Godzilla</a></i> (1954).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup> These films have had a profound influence on world cinema. In particular, Kurosawa's <i>Seven Samurai</i> has been remade several times as <a href="/wiki/Western_(genre)" title="Western (genre)">Western films</a>, such as <i><a href="/wiki/The_Magnificent_Seven" title="The Magnificent Seven">The Magnificent Seven</a></i> (1960) and <i><a href="/wiki/Battle_Beyond_the_Stars" title="Battle Beyond the Stars">Battle Beyond the Stars</a></i> (1980), and has also inspired several <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bollywood" title="Bollywood">Bollywood</a> films, such as <i><a href="/wiki/Sholay" title="Sholay">Sholay</a></i> (1975) and <i><a href="/wiki/China_Gate_(1998_film)" title="China Gate (1998 film)">China Gate</a></i> (1998). <i>Rashomon</i> was also remade as <i><a href="/wiki/The_Outrage" title="The Outrage">The Outrage</a></i> (1964), and inspired films with "<a href="/wiki/Rashomon_effect" title="Rashomon effect">Rashomon effect</a>" storytelling methods, such as <i><a href="/wiki/Andha_Naal" title="Andha Naal">Andha Naal</a></i> (1954), <i><a href="/wiki/The_Usual_Suspects" title="The Usual Suspects">The Usual Suspects</a></i> (1995) and <i><a href="/wiki/Hero_(2002_film)" title="Hero (2002 film)">Hero</a></i> (2002). <i>The Hidden Fortress</i> was also the inspiration behind <a href="/wiki/George_Lucas" title="George Lucas">George Lucas</a>' <i><a href="/wiki/Star_Wars_(film)" title="Star Wars (film)">Star Wars</a></i> (1977). Other famous Japanese filmmakers from this period include <a href="/wiki/Kenji_Mizoguchi" title="Kenji Mizoguchi">Kenji Mizoguchi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mikio_Naruse" title="Mikio Naruse">Mikio Naruse</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hiroshi_Inagaki" title="Hiroshi Inagaki">Hiroshi Inagaki</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nagisa_Oshima" title="Nagisa Oshima">Nagisa Oshima</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lee_100-2"><a href="#cite_note-Lee-100">[100]</a></sup> Japanese cinema later became one of the main inspirations behind the <a href="/wiki/New_Hollywood" title="New Hollywood">New Hollywood</a> movement of the 1960s to 1980s.
</p><p>During <a href="/wiki/Cinema_of_India" title="Cinema of India">Indian cinema</a>'s 'Golden Age' of the 1950s, it was producing 200 films annually, while <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parallel_Cinema" title="Parallel Cinema">Indian independent films</a> gained greater recognition through international <a href="/wiki/Film_festival" title="Film festival">film festivals</a>. One of the most famous was <i><a href="/wiki/The_Apu_Trilogy" title="The Apu Trilogy">The Apu Trilogy</a></i> (1955–1959) from critically acclaimed <a href="/wiki/Cinema_of_West_Bengal" title="Cinema
