of West Bengal">Bengali film</a> director <a href="/wiki/Satyajit_Ray" title="Satyajit Ray">Satyajit Ray</a>, whose films had a profound influence on world cinema, with directors such as Akira Kurosawa,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Martin_Scorsese" title="Martin Scorsese">Martin Scorsese</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/James_Ivory_(director)" title="James Ivory (director)">James Ivory</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Abbas_Kiarostami" title="Abbas Kiarostami">Abbas Kiarostami</a>, <a href="/wiki/Elia_Kazan" title="Elia Kazan">Elia Kazan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fran%C3%A7ois_Truffaut" title="François Truffaut">François Truffaut</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Steven_Spielberg" title="Steven Spielberg">Steven Spielberg</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-unmaderay_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-unmaderay-108">[108]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IMDbRay_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-IMDbRay-109">[109]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UCSCcurrents_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-UCSCcurrents-110">[110]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Carlos_Saura" title="Carlos Saura">Carlos Saura</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Jean-Luc_Godard" title="Jean-Luc Godard">Jean-Luc Godard</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Isao_Takahata" title="Isao Takahata">Isao Takahata</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Gregory_Nava" title="Gregory Nava">Gregory Nava</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ira_Sachs" title="Ira Sachs">Ira Sachs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wes_Anderson" title="Wes Anderson">Wes Anderson</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_Statesman_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-The_Statesman-114">[114]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Danny_Boyle" title="Danny Boyle">Danny Boyle</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jivani_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jivani-115">[115]</a></sup> being influenced by his cinematic style. According to Michael Sragow of <i><a href="/wiki/The_Atlantic" title="The Atlantic">The Atlantic Monthly</a></i>, the "youthful <a href="/wiki/Coming_of_age" title="Coming of age">coming-of-age</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Drama_film" title="Drama film">dramas</a> that have flooded art houses since the mid-fifties owe a tremendous debt to the Apu trilogy".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Subrata_Mitra" title="Subrata Mitra">Subrata Mitra</a>'s cinematographic technique of <a href="/wiki/Reflector_(photography)#Bounce_lighting" title="Reflector (photography)">bounce lighting</a> also originates from <i>The Apu Trilogy</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cinematographers_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-cinematographers-117">[117]</a></sup> Other famous Indian filmmakers from this period include <a href="/wiki/Guru_Dutt" title="Guru Dutt">Guru Dutt</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lee_100-3"><a href="#cite_note-Lee-100">[100]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Ritwik_Ghatak" title="Ritwik Ghatak">Ritwik Ghatak</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Totaro_101-2"><a href="#cite_note-Totaro-101">[101]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Mrinal_Sen" title="Mrinal Sen">Mrinal Sen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Raj_Kapoor" title="Raj Kapoor">Raj Kapoor</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bimal_Roy" title="Bimal Roy">Bimal Roy</a>, <a href="/wiki/K._Asif" title="K. Asif">K. Asif</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mehboob_Khan" title="Mehboob Khan">Mehboob Khan</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cinemacom_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cinemacom-118">[118]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Cinema_of_South_Korea" title="Cinema of South Korea">cinema of South Korea</a> also experienced a 'Golden Age' in the 1950s, beginning with director Lee Kyu-hwan's tremendously successful remake of <i>Chunhyang-jon</i> (1955).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marshall_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-Marshall-119">[119]</a></sup> That year also saw the release of <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yangsan_Province_(film)" title="Yangsan Province (film)">Yangsan Province</a></i> by the renowned director, <a href="/wiki/Kim_Ki-young" title="Kim Ki-young">Kim Ki-young</a>, marking the beginning of his productive career. Both the quality and quantity of filmmaking had increased rapidly by the end of the 1950s. South Korean films, such as Lee Byeong-il's 1956 comedy <i>Sijibganeun nal (The Wedding Day)</i>, had begun winning international awards. In contrast to the beginning of the 1950s, when only 5 films were made per year, 111 films were produced in South Korea in 1959.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup>
</p><p>The 1950s was also a 'Golden Age' for <a href="/wiki/Cinema_of_the_Philippines" title="Cinema of the Philippines">Philippine cinema</a>, with the emergence of more artistic and mature films, and significant improvement in cinematic techniques among filmmakers. The studio system produced frenetic activity in the local film industry as many films were made annually and several local talents started to earn recognition abroad. The premiere Philippine directors of the era included <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gerardo_de_Leon" title="Gerardo de Leon">Gerardo de Leon</a>, <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Gregorio_Fern%C3%A1ndez_(director)&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Gregorio Fernández (director) (page does not exist)">Gregorio Fernández</a>, <a href="/wiki/Eddie_Romero" title="Eddie Romero">Eddie Romero</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lamberto_Avellana" title="Lamberto Avellana">Lamberto Avellana</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cirio_Santiago" title="Cirio Santiago">Cirio Santiago</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-curtain_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-curtain-121">[121]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="1960s">1960s</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_film&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: 1960s">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/1960s_in_film" title="1960s in film">1960s in film</a></div>
<p>During the 1960s, the studio system in <a href="/wiki/Cinema_of_the_United_States" title="Cinema of the United States">Hollywood</a> declined, because many films were now being made on location in other countries, or using studio facilities abroad, such as <a href="/wiki/Pinewood_Studios" title="Pinewood Studios">Pinewood</a> in the UK and <a href="/wiki/Cinecitt%C3%A0" title="Cinecittà">Cinecittà</a> in Rome. "Hollywood" films were still largely aimed at family audiences, and it was often the more old-fashioned films that produced the studios' biggest successes. Productions like <i><a href="/wiki/Mary_Poppins_(film)" title="Mary Poppins (film)">Mary Poppins</a></i> (1964), <i><a href="/wiki/My_Fair_Lady_(film)" title="My Fair Lady (film)">My Fair Lady</a></i> (1964) and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Sound_of_Music_(film)" title="The Sound of Music (film)">The Sound of Music</a></i> (1965) were among the biggest money-makers of the decade. The growth in independent producers and production companies, and the increase in the power of individual actors also contributed to the decline of traditional Hollywood studio production.
</p><p>There was also an increasing awareness of foreign language cinema in America during this period. During the late 1950s and 1960s, the <a href="/wiki/French_New_Wave" title="French New Wave">French New Wave</a> directors such as <a href="/wiki/Fran%C3%A7ois_Truffaut" title="François Truffaut">François Truffaut</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jean-Luc_Godard" title="Jean-Luc Godard">Jean-Luc Godard</a> produced films such as <i><a href="/wiki/The_400_Blows" title="The 400 Blows">Les quatre cents coups</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Breathless_(1960_film)" title="Breathless (1960 film)">Breathless</a></i> and <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jules_et_Jim" title="Jules et Jim">Jules et Jim</a></i> which broke the rules of Hollywood cinema's <a href="/wiki/Narrative_structure" title="Narrative structure">narrative structure</a>. As well, audiences were becoming aware of Italian films like <a href="/wiki/Federico_Fellini" title="Federico Fellini">Federico Fellini</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/La_Dolce_Vita" title="La Dolce Vita">La Dolce Vita</a></i> (1960), <i><a href="/wiki/8%C2%BD" title="8½">8½</a></i> (1963) and the stark dramas of Sweden's <a href="/wiki/Ingmar_Bergman" title="Ingmar Bergman">Ingmar Bergman</a>.
</p><p>In Britain, the "Free Cinema" of <a href="/wiki/Lindsay_Anderson" title="Lindsay Anderson">Lindsay Anderson</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tony_Richardson" title="Tony Richardson">Tony Richardson</a> and others lead to a group of realistic and innovative dramas including <i><a href="/wiki/Saturday_Night_and_Sunday_Morning_(film)" title="Saturday Night and Sunday Morning (film)">Saturday Night and Sunday Morning</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/A_Kind_of_Loving_(film)" title="A Kind of Loving (film)">A Kind of Loving</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/This_Sporting_Life" title="This Sporting Life">This Sporting Life</a></i>. Other British films such as <i><a href="/wiki/Repulsion_(film)" title="Repulsion (film)">Repulsion</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Darling_(1965_film)" title="Darling (1965 film)">Darling</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Alfie_(1966_film)" title="Alfie (1966 film)">Alfie</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Blowup" title="Blowup">Blowup</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Georgy_Girl" title="Georgy Girl">Georgy Girl</a></i> (all in 1965–1966) helped to reduce prohibitions of sex and nudity on screen, while the casual sex and violence of the <a href="/wiki/James_Bond" title="James Bond">James Bond</a> films, beginning with <i><a href="/wiki/Dr._No_(film)" title="Dr. No (film)">Dr. No</a></i> in 1962 would render the series popular worldwide.
</p><p>During the 1960s, <a href="/wiki/Ousmane_Semb%C3%A8ne" title="Ousmane Sembène">Ousmane Sembène</a> produced several French- and <a href="/wiki/Wolof_language" title="Wolof language">Wolof-language</a> films and became the "father" of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/African_Cinema" title="African Cinema">African Cinema</a>. In Latin America, the dominance of the "Hollywood" model was challenged by many film makers. <a href="/wiki/Fernando_Solanas" title="Fernando Solanas">Fernando Solanas</a> and <a href="/wiki/Octavio_Getino" title="Octavio Getino">Octavio Getino</a> called for a politically engaged <a href="/wiki/Third_Cinema" title="Third Cinema">Third Cinema</a> in contrast to Hollywood and the European <a href="/wiki/Auteur" title="Auteur">auteur</a> cinema.
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a>, the golden age of <a href="/wiki/Cinema_of_Egypt" title="Cinema of Egypt">Egyptian cinema</a> continued in the 1960s at the hands of many directors, and Egyptian cinema greatly appreciated women at that time, such as <a href="/wiki/Soad_Hosny" title="Soad Hosny">Soad Hosny</a>. The Zulfikar brothers; <a href="/wiki/Ezz_El-Dine_Zulficar" title="Ezz El-Dine Zulficar">Ezz El-Dine Zulfikar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Salah_Zulfikar" title="Salah Zulfikar">Salah Zulfikar</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mahmoud_Zulfikar" title="Mahmoud Zulfikar">Mahmoud Zulfikar</a> were on a date with many productions,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup> including <a href="/wiki/Ezz_El-Dine_Zulficar" title="Ezz El-Dine Zulficar">Ezz El Dine Zulfikar's</a> <i><a href="/wiki/The_River_of_Love_(film)" title="The River of Love (film)">The River of Love</a></i> (1960),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Mahmoud_Zulfikar" title="Mahmoud Zulfikar">Mahmoud Zulfikar's</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Soft_Hands_(film)" title="Soft Hands (film)">Soft Hands</a></i> (1964), and <i><a href="/wiki/Aghla_Min_Hayati" title="Aghla Min Hayati">Dearer Than My Life</a></i> (1965) starring <a href="/wiki/Salah_Zulfikar" title="Salah Zulfikar">Salah Zulfikar</a> and Salah Zulfikar's production; <i><a href="/wiki/My_Wife,_the_Director_General" title="My Wife, the Director General">My Wife, the Director General</a></i> (1966)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup> as well as <a href="/wiki/Youssef_Chahine" title="Youssef Chahine">Youssef Chahine's</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Saladin_the_Victorious" title="Saladin the Victorious">Saladin</a></i> (1963).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup>
</p><p>Further, the nuclear paranoia of the age, and the threat of an apocalyptic nuclear exchange (like the 1962 close-call with the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">USSR</a> during the <a href="/wiki/Cuban_Missile_Crisis" title="Cuban Missile Crisis">Cuban Missile Crisis</a>) prompted a reaction within the film community as well. Films like <a href="/wiki/Stanley_Kubrick" title="Stanley Kubrick">Stanley Kubrick</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Dr._Strangelove" title="Dr. Strangelove">Dr. Strangelove</a></i> and <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fail-Safe_(1964_film)" title="Fail-Safe (1964 film)">Fail Safe</a></i> with <a href="/wiki/Henry_Fonda" title="Henry Fonda">Henry Fonda</a> were produced in a Hollywood that was once known for its overt patriotism and wartime propaganda.
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Documentary_film" title="Documentary film">documentary film</a> the sixties saw the blossoming of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Direct_Cinema" title="Direct Cinema">Direct Cinema</a>, an observational style of film making as well as the advent of more overtly partisan films like <i><a href="/wiki/In_the_Year_of_the_Pig" title="In the Year of the Pig">In the Year of the Pig</a></i> about the <a href="/wiki/Vietnam_War" title="Vietnam War">Vietnam War</a> by <a href="/wiki/Emile_de_Antonio" title="Emile de Antonio">Emile de Antonio</a>. By the late 1960s however, Hollywood filmmakers were beginning to create more innovative and groundbreaking films that reflected the social revolution taken over much of the western world such as <i><a href="/wiki/Bonnie_and_Clyde_(film)" title="Bonnie and Clyde (film)">Bonnie and Clyde</a></i> (1967), <i><a href="/wiki/The_Graduate" title="The Graduate">The Graduate</a></i> (1967), <i><a href="/wiki/2001:_A_Space_Odyssey_(film)" title="2001: A Space Odyssey (film)">2001: A Space Odyssey</a></i> (1968), <i><a href="/wiki/Rosemary%27s_Baby_(film)" title="Rosemary's Baby (film)">Rosemary's Baby</a></i> (1968), <i><a href="/wiki/Midnight_Cowboy" title="Midnight Cowboy">Midnight Cowboy</a></i> (1969), <i><a href="/wiki/Easy_Rider" title="Easy Rider">Easy Rider</a></i> (1969) and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Wild_Bunch" title="The Wild Bunch">The Wild Bunch</a></i> (1969). <i><a href="/wiki/Bonnie_and_Clyde_(film)" title="Bonnie and Clyde (film)">Bonnie and Clyde</a></i> is often considered the beginning of the so-called <a href="/wiki/New_Hollywood" title="New Hollywood">New Hollywood</a>.
</p><p>In Japanese cinema, Academy Award-winning director <a href="/wiki/Akira_Kurosawa" title="Akira Kurosawa">Akira Kurosawa</a> produced <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yojimbo_(film)" title="Yojimbo (film)">Yojimbo</a></i> (1961), which like his previous films also had a profound influence around the world. The influence of this film is most apparent in <a href="/wiki/Sergio_Leone" title="Sergio Leone">Sergio Leone</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/A_Fistful_of_Dollars" title="A Fistful of Dollars">A Fistful of Dollars</a></i> (1964) and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Walter_Hill_(filmmaker)" title="Walter Hill (filmmaker)">Walter Hill</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Last_Man_Standing_(1996_film)" title="Last Man Standing (1996 film)">Last Man Standing</a></i> (1996). <i>Yojimbo</i> was also the origin of the "<a href="/wiki/Man_with_No_Name" title="Man with No Name">Man with No Name</a>" trend.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="1970s">1970s</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_film&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: 1970s">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/1970s_in_film" title="1970s in film">1970s in film</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/New_Hollywood" title="New Hollywood">New Hollywood</a> was the period following the decline of the <a href="/wiki/Studio_system" title="Studio system">studio system</a> during the 1950s and 1960s and the end of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Production_code" title="Production code">production code</a>, (which was replaced in 1968 by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/MPAA_film_rating_system" title="MPAA film rating system">MPAA film rating system</a>). During the 1970s, filmmakers increasingly depicted explicit sexual content and showed gunfight and battle scenes that included graphic images of bloody deaths –  a good example of this is <a href="/wiki/Wes_Craven" title="Wes Craven">Wes Craven</a>'s <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Last_House_on_the_Left_(1972_film)" title="The Last House on the Left (1972 film)">The Last House on the Left</a></i> (1972).
</p><p>Post-classical cinema is the changing methods of storytelling of the New Hollywood producers. The new methods of <a href="/wiki/Drama" title="Drama">drama</a> and characterization played upon audience expectations acquired during the classical/Golden Age period: story chronology may be scrambled, storylines may feature unsettling "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Twist_ending" title="Twist ending">twist endings</a>", main characters may behave in a morally ambiguous fashion, and the lines between the <a href="/wiki/Antagonist" title="Antagonist">antagonist</a> and <a href="/wiki/Protagonist" title="Protagonist">protagonist</a> may be blurred. The beginnings of post-classical storytelling may be seen in 1940s and 1950s <a href="/wiki/Film_noir" title="Film noir">film noir</a> films, in films such as <i><a href="/wiki/Rebel_Without_a_Cause" title="Rebel Without a Cause">Rebel Without a Cause</a></i> (1955), and in Hitchcock's <i><a href="/wiki/Psycho_(1960_film)" title="Psycho (1960 film)">Psycho</a></i>. 1971 marked the release of controversial films like <i><a href="/wiki/Straw_Dogs_(1971_film)" title="Straw Dogs (1971 film)">Straw Dogs</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/A_Clockwork_Orange_(film)" title="A Clockwork Orange (film)">A Clockwork Orange</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/The_French_Connection_(film)" title="The French Connection (film)">The French Connection</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Dirty_Harry" title="Dirty Harry">Dirty Harry</a></i>. This sparked heated controversy over the perceived escalation of violence in cinema.
</p><p>During the 1970s, a new group of American filmmakers emerged, such as <a href="/wiki/Martin_Scorsese" title="Martin Scorsese">Martin Scorsese</a>, <a href="/wiki/Francis_Ford_Coppola" title="Francis Ford Coppola">Francis Ford Coppola</a>, <a href="/wiki/George_Lucas" title="George Lucas">George Lucas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Woody_Allen" title="Woody Allen">Woody Allen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Terrence_Malick" title="Terrence Malick">Terrence Malick</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Robert_Altman" title="Robert Altman">Robert Altman</a>. This coincided with the increasing popularity of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Auteur_theory" title="Auteur theory">auteur theory</a> in film literature and the media, which posited that a film director's films express their personal vision and creative insights. The development of the auteur style of filmmaking helped to give these directors far greater control over their projects than would have been possible in earlier eras. This led to some great critical and commercial successes, like Scorsese's <i><a href="/wiki/Taxi_Driver" title="Taxi Driver">Taxi Driver</a></i>, Coppola's <i><a href="/wiki/The_Godfather" title="The Godfather">The Godfather</a></i> films, <a href="/wiki/William_Friedkin" title="William Friedkin">William Friedkin</a>'s <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Exorcist_(film)" title="The Exorcist (film)">The Exorcist</a></i>, Altman's <i><a href="/wiki/Nashville_(film)" title="Nashville (film)">Nashville</a></i>, Allen's <i><a href="/wiki/Annie_Hall" title="Annie Hall">Annie Hall</a></i> and <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Manhattan_(film)" title="Manhattan (film)">Manhattan</a></i>, Malick's <i><a href="/wiki/Badlands_(film)" title="Badlands (film)">Badlands</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Days_of_Heaven" title="Days of Heaven">Days of Heaven</a></i>, and Polish immigrant <a href="/wiki/Roman_Polanski" title="Roman Polanski">Roman Polanski</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Chinatown_(1974_film)" title="Chinatown (1974 film)">Chinatown</a></i>. It also, however, resulted in some failures, including <a href="/wiki/Peter_Bogdanovich" title="Peter Bogdanovich">Peter Bogdanovich</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/At_Long_Last_Love" title="At Long Last Love">At Long Last Love</a></i> and <a href="/wiki/Michael_Cimino" title="Michael Cimino">Michael Cimino</a>'s hugely expensive Western epic <i><a href="/wiki/Heaven%27s_Gate_(film)" title="Heaven's Gate (film)">Heaven's Gate</a></i>, which helped to bring about the demise of its backer, <a href="/wiki/United_Artists" title="United Artists">United Artists</a>.
</p><p>The financial disaster of <i>Heaven's Gate</i> marked the end of the visionary "auteur" directors of the "New Hollywood", who had unrestrained creative and financial freedom to develop films. The phenomenal success in the 1970s of <a href="/wiki/Steven_Spielberg" title="Steven Spielberg">Spielberg</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Jaws_(film)" title="Jaws (film)">Jaws</a></i> originated the concept of the modern "<a href="/wiki/Blockbuster_(entertainment)" title="Blockbuster (entertainment)">blockbuster</a>". However, the enormous success of George Lucas' 1977 film <i><a href="/wiki/Star_Wars_(film)" title="Star Wars (film)">Star Wars</a></i> led to much more than just the popularization of blockbuster film-making. The film's revolutionary use of special effects, sound editing and music had led it to become widely regarded as one of the single most important films in the medium's history, as well as the most influential film of the 1970s. Hollywood studios increasingly focused on producing a smaller number of very large budget films with massive marketing and promotional campaigns. This trend had already been foreshadowed by the commercial success of <a href="/wiki/Disaster_film" title="Disaster film">disaster films</a> such as <i><a href="/wiki/The_Poseidon_Adventure_(1972_film)" title="The Poseidon Adventure (1972 film)">The Poseidon Adventure</a></i> and <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Towering_Inferno_(film)" title="The Towering Inferno (film)">The Towering Inferno</a></i>.
</p><p>During the mid-1970s, more pornographic theatres, euphemistically called "adult cinemas", were established, and the legal production of <a href="/wiki/Hardcore_pornography" title="Hardcore pornography">hardcore pornographic</a> films began. Porn films such as <i><a href="/wiki/Deep_Throat_(film)" title="Deep Throat (film)">Deep Throat</a></i> and its star <a href="/wiki/Linda_Lovelace" title="Linda Lovelace">Linda Lovelace</a> became something of a popular culture phenomenon and resulted in a spate of similar sex films. The porn cinemas finally died out during the 1980s, when the popularization of the home <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/VCR" title="VCR">VCR</a> and pornography videotapes allowed audiences to watch sex films at home. In the early 1970s, English-language audiences became more aware of the new <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/West_German" title="West German">West German</a> cinema, with <a href="/wiki/Werner_Herzog" title="Werner Herzog">Werner Herzog</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rainer_Werner_Fassbinder" title="Rainer Werner Fassbinder">Rainer Werner Fassbinder</a> and <a href="/wiki/Wim_Wenders" title="Wim Wenders">Wim Wenders</a> among its leading exponents.
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/World_cinema" title="World cinema">world cinema</a>, the 1970s saw a dramatic increase in the popularity of <a href="/wiki/Martial_arts_film" title="Martial arts film">martial arts films</a>, largely due to its reinvention by <a href="/wiki/Bruce_Lee" title="Bruce Lee">Bruce Lee</a>, who departed from the artistic style of traditional <a href="/wiki/Chinese_martial_arts" title="Chinese martial arts">Chinese martial arts</a> films and added a much greater sense of realism to them with his <a href="/wiki/Jeet_Kune_Do" title="Jeet Kune Do">Jeet Kune Do</a> style. This began with <i><a href="/wiki/The_Big_Boss" title="The Big Boss">The Big Boss</a></i> (1971), which was a major success across <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Asian_cinema" title="Asian cinema">Asia</a>. However, he didn't gain fame in the <a href="/wiki/Western_world" title="Western world">Western world</a> until shortly after his death in 1973, when <i><a href="/wiki/Enter_the_Dragon" title="Enter the Dragon">Enter the Dragon</a></i> was released. The film went on to become the most successful martial arts film in cinematic history, popularized the martial arts film genre across the world, and cemented Bruce Lee's status as a cultural icon. <a href="/wiki/Hong_Kong_action_cinema" title="Hong Kong action cinema">Hong Kong action cinema</a>, however, was in decline due to a wave of "<a href="/wiki/Bruceploitation" title="Bruceploitation">Bruceploitation</a>" films. This trend eventually came to an end in 1978 with the martial arts <a href="/wiki/Comedy_film" title="Comedy film">comedy films</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/Snake_in_the_Eagle%27s_Shadow" title="Snake in the Eagle's Shadow">Snake in the Eagle's Shadow</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Drunken_Master" title="Drunken Master">Drunken Master</a></i>, directed by <a href="/wiki/Yuen_Woo-ping" title="Yuen Woo-ping">Yuen Woo-ping</a> and starring <a href="/wiki/Jackie_Chan" title="Jackie Chan">Jackie Chan</a>, laying the foundations for the rise of Hong Kong action cinema in the 1980s.
</p><p>While the <a href="/wiki/Musical_film" title="Musical film">musical film</a> genre had declined in Hollywood by this time, musical films were quickly gaining popularity in the <a href="/wiki/Cinema_of_India" title="Cinema of India">cinema of India</a>, where the term "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bollywood" title="Bollywood">Bollywood</a>" was coined for the growing <a href="/wiki/Hindi" title="Hindi">Hindi</a> film industry in <a href="/wiki/Mumbai" title="Mumbai">Bombay</a> (now Mumbai) that ended up dominating <a href="/wiki/South_Asian_cinema" title="South Asian cinema">South Asian cinema</a>, overtaking the more critically acclaimed <a href="/wiki/Cinema_of_West_Bengal" title="Cinema of West Bengal">Bengali film industry</a> in popularity. Hindi filmmakers combined the Hollywood musical formula with the conventions of ancient <a href="/wiki/Theatre_of_India" title="Theatre of India">Indian theatre</a> to create a new film genre called "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Masala_(film_genre)" title="Masala (film genre)">Masala</a>", which dominated Indian cinema throughout the late 20th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup> These "Masala" films portrayed <a href="/wiki/Action_film" title="Action film">action</a>, comedy, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Drama_film" title="Drama film">drama</a>, <a href="/wiki/Romance_film" title="Romance film">romance</a> and <a href="/wiki/Melodrama" title="Melodrama">melodrama</a> all at once, with "<a href="/wiki/Filmi" title="Filmi">filmi</a>" song and dance routines thrown in. This trend began with films directed by <a href="/wiki/Manmohan_Desai" title="Manmohan Desai">Manmohan Desai</a> and starring <a href="/wiki/Amitabh_Bachchan" title="Amitabh Bachchan">Amitabh Bachchan</a>, who remains one of the most popular <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Film_star" title="Film star">film stars</a> in South Asia. The most popular Indian film of all time was <i><a href="/wiki/Sholay" title="Sholay">Sholay</a></i> (1975), a "Masala" film inspired by a real-life <a href="/wiki/Dacoity" title="Dacoity">dacoit</a> as well as Kurosawa's <i><a href="/wiki/Seven_Samurai" title="Seven Samurai">Seven Samurai</a></i> and the <a href="/wiki/Spaghetti_Western" title="Spaghetti Western">Spaghetti Westerns</a>.
</p><p>The end of the decade saw the first major international marketing of <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">Australian</a> cinema, as <a href="/wiki/Peter_Weir" title="Peter Weir">Peter Weir</a>'s films <i><a href="/wiki/Picnic_at_Hanging_Rock_(film)" title="Picnic at Hanging Rock (film)">Picnic at Hanging Rock</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Last_Wave" title="The Last Wave">The Last Wave</a></i> and <a href="/wiki/Fred_Schepisi" title="Fred Schepisi">Fred Schepisi</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/The_Chant_of_Jimmie_Blacksmith_(film)" title="The Chant of Jimmie Blacksmith (film)">The Chant of Jimmie Blacksmith</a></i> gained critical acclaim. In 1979, Australian filmmaker <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/George_Miller_(producer)" title="George Miller (producer)">George Miller</a> also garnered international attention for his violent, low-budget action film <i><a href="/wiki/Mad_Max_(film)" title="Mad Max (film)">Mad Max</a></i>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="1980s">1980s</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_film&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: 1980s">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/1980s_in_film" title="1980s in film">1980s in film</a></div>
<p>During the 1980s, audiences began increasingly watching films on their home <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/VCR" title="VCR">VCRs</a>. In the early part of that decade, the <a href="/wiki/Film_studio" title="Film studio">film studios</a> tried legal action to ban home ownership of VCRs as a violation of <a href="/wiki/Copyright" title="Copyright">copyright</a>, which proved unsuccessful. Eventually, the sale and rental of films on <a href="/wiki/Home_video" title="Home video">home video</a> became a significant "second venue" for exhibition of films, and an additional source of revenue for the film industries. <a href="/wiki/Direct-to-video" title="Direct-to-video">Direct-to-video</a> (niche) markets usually offered lower quality, cheap productions that were not deemed very suitable for the general audiences of television and theatrical releases.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/George_Lucas" title="George Lucas">Lucas</a>–<a href="/wiki/Steven_Spielberg" title="Steven Spielberg">Spielberg</a> combine would dominate "Hollywood" cinema for much of the 1980s, and lead to much imitation. Two follow-ups to <i><a href="/wiki/Star_Wars_(film)" title="Star Wars (film)">Star Wars</a></i>, three to <i><a href="/wiki/Jaws_(film)" title="Jaws (film)">Jaws</a></i>, and three <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indiana_Jones_(franchise)" title="Indiana Jones (franchise)">Indiana Jones</a></i> films helped to make sequels of successful films more of an expectation than ever before. Lucas also launched <a href="/wiki/THX" title="THX">THX Ltd</a>, a division of <a href="/wiki/Lucasfilm" title="Lucasfilm">Lucasfilm</a> in 1982,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup> while Spielberg enjoyed one of the decade's greatest successes in <i><a href="/wiki/E.T._the_Extra-Terrestrial" title="E.T. the Extra-Terrestrial">E.T. the Extra-Terrestrial</a></i> the same year. 1982 also saw the release of Disney's <i><a href="/wiki/Tron" title="Tron">Tron</a></i> which was one of the first films from a major studio to use <a href="/wiki/Computer_graphics" title="Computer graphics">computer graphics</a> extensively. American independent cinema struggled more during the decade, although <a href="/wiki/Martin_Scorsese" title="Martin Scorsese">Martin Scorsese</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Raging_Bull" title="Raging Bull">Raging Bull</a></i> (1980), <i><a href="/wiki/After_Hours_(film)" title="After Hours (film)">After Hours</a></i> (1985), and <i><a href="/wiki/The_King_of_Comedy_(film)" title="The King of Comedy (film)">The King of Comedy</a></i> (1983) helped to establish him as one of the most critically acclaimed American film makers of the era. Also during 1983 <i><a href="/wiki/Scarface_(1983_film)" title="Scarface (1983 film)">Scarface</a></i> was released, which was very profitable and resulted in even greater fame for its leading actor <a href="/wiki/Al_Pacino" title="Al Pacino">Al Pacino</a>. Probably<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Avoid_weasel_words" title="Wikipedia:Avoid weasel words"><span title="Who says this? (August 2018)">weasel words</span></a></i>]</sup> the most successful film commercially was <a href="/wiki/Tim_Burton" title="Tim Burton">Tim Burton</a>'s 1989 version of <a href="/wiki/Bob_Kane" title="Bob Kane">Bob Kane</a>'s creation, <i><a href="/wiki/Batman_(1989_film)" title="Batman (1989 film)">Batman</a></i>, which broke box-office records. <a href="/wiki/Jack_Nicholson" title="Jack Nicholson">Jack Nicholson</a>'s portrayal of the demented <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Joker_(comics)" title="Joker (comics)">Joker</a> earned him a total of $60,000,000 after figuring in his percentage of the gross.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>British cinema was given a boost during the early 1980s by the arrival of <a href="/wiki/David_Puttnam" title="David Puttnam">David Puttnam</a>'s company <a href="/wiki/Goldcrest_Films" title="Goldcrest Films">Goldcrest Films</a>. The films <i><a href="/wiki/Chariots_of_Fire" title="Chariots of Fire">Chariots of Fire</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Gandhi_(film)" title="Gandhi (film)">Gandhi</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Killing_Fields_(film)" title="The Killing Fields (film)">The Killing Fields</a></i> and <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/A_Room_with_a_View_(1986_film)" title="A Room with a View (1986 film)">A Room with a View</a></i> appealed to a "middlebrow" audience which was increasingly being ignored by the major Hollywood studios. While the films of the 1970s had helped to define modern <a href="/wiki/Blockbuster_(entertainment)" title="Blockbuster (entertainment)">blockbuster</a> motion pictures, the way "Hollywood" released its films would now change. Films, for the most part, would premiere in a wider number of theatres, although, to this day, some films still premiere using the route of the <a href="/wiki/Roadshow_theatrical_release" title="Roadshow theatrical release">limited/roadshow release system</a>. Against some expectations, the rise of the multiplex cinema did not allow less mainstream films to be shown, but simply allowed the major blockbusters to be given an even greater number of screenings. However, films that had been overlooked in cinemas were increasingly being given a second chance on home video.
</p><p>During the 1980s, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Japanese_cinema" title="Japanese cinema">Japanese cinema</a> experienced a revival, largely due to the success of <a href="/wiki/Anime" title="Anime">anime</a> films. At the beginning of the 1980s, <i><a href="/wiki/Space_Battleship_Yamato" title="Space Battleship Yamato">Space Battleship Yamato</a></i> (1973) and <i><a href="/wiki/Mobile_Suit_Gundam" title="Mobile Suit Gundam">Mobile Suit Gundam</a></i> (1979), both of which were unsuccessful as television series, were remade as films and became hugely successful in Japan. In particular, <i>Mobile Suit Gundam</i> sparked the <a href="/wiki/Gundam" title="Gundam">Gundam</a> franchise of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Real_Robot" title="Real Robot">Real Robot</a> <a href="/wiki/Mecha" title="Mecha">mecha</a> anime. The success of <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Super_Dimension_Fortress_Macross:_Do_You_Remember_Love%3F" title="The Super Dimension Fortress Macross: Do You Remember Love?">Macross: Do You Remember Love?</a></i> also sparked a <a href="/wiki/Macross" title="Macross">Macross</a> franchise of mecha anime. This was also the decade when <a href="/wiki/Studio_Ghibli" title="Studio Ghibli">Studio Ghibli</a> was founded. The studio produced <a href="/wiki/Hayao_Miyazaki" title="Hayao Miyazaki">Hayao Miyazaki</a>'s first <a href="/wiki/Fantasy_film" title="Fantasy film">fantasy films</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/Nausica%C3%A4_of_the_Valley_of_the_Wind_(film)" title="Nausicaä of the Valley of the Wind (film)">Nausicaä of the Valley of the Wind</a></i> (1984) and <i><a href="/wiki/Castle_in_the_Sky" title="Castle in the Sky">Castle in the Sky</a></i> (1986), as well as <a href="/wiki/Isao_Takahata" title="Isao Takahata">Isao Takahata</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Grave_of_the_Fireflies" title="Grave of the Fireflies">Grave of the Fireflies</a></i> (1988), all of which were very successful in Japan and received worldwide critical acclaim. <a href="/wiki/Original_video_animation" title="Original video animation">Original video animation</a> (OVA) films also began during this decade; the most influential of these early OVA films was <a href="/wiki/Noboru_Ishiguro" title="Noboru Ishiguro">Noboru Ishiguro</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Cyberpunk" title="Cyberpunk">cyberpunk</a> film <i><a href="/wiki/Megazone_23" title="Megazone 23">Megazone 23</a></i> (1985). The most famous anime film of this decade was <a href="/wiki/Katsuhiro_Otomo" title="Katsuhiro Otomo">Katsuhiro Otomo</a>'s cyberpunk film <i><a href="/wiki/Akira_(1988_film)" title="Akira (1988 film)">Akira</a></i> (1988), which although initially unsuccessful at Japanese theaters, went on to become an international success.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Hong_Kong_action_cinema" title="Hong Kong action cinema">Hong Kong action cinema</a>, which was in a state of decline due to endless <a href="/wiki/Bruceploitation" title="Bruceploitation">Bruceploitation</a> films after the death of <a href="/wiki/Bruce_Lee" title="Bruce Lee">Bruce Lee</a>, also experienced a revival in the 1980s, largely due to the reinvention of the <a href="/wiki/Action_film" title="Action film">action film</a> genre by <a href="/wiki/Jackie_Chan" title="Jackie Chan">Jackie Chan</a>. He had previously combined the <a href="/wiki/Comedy_film" title="Comedy film">comedy film</a> and <a href="/wiki/Martial_arts_film" title="Martial arts film">martial arts film</a> genres successfully in the 1978 films <i><a href="/wiki/Snake_in_the_Eagle%27s_Shadow" title="Snake in the Eagle's Shadow">Snake in the Eagle's Shadow</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Drunken_Master" title="Drunken Master">Drunken Master</a></i>. The next step he took was in combining this comedy martial arts genre with a new emphasis on elaborate and highly dangerous <a href="/wiki/Stunt" title="Stunt">stunts</a>, reminiscent of the <a href="/wiki/Silent_film" title="Silent film">silent film</a> era. The first film in this new style of action cinema was <i><a href="/wiki/Project_A_(film)" title="Project A (film)">Project A</a></i> (1983), which saw the formation of the <a href="/wiki/Jackie_Chan_Stunt_Team" title="Jackie Chan Stunt Team">Jackie Chan Stunt Team</a> as well as the "Three Brothers" (Chan, <a href="/wiki/Sammo_Hung" title="Sammo Hung">Sammo Hung</a> and <a href="/wiki/Yuen_Biao" title="Yuen Biao">Yuen Biao</a>). The film added elaborate, dangerous stunts to the fights and <a href="/wiki/Slapstick" title="Slapstick">slapstick</a> humor, and became a huge success throughout the <a href="/wiki/Far_East" title="Far East">Far East</a>. As a result, Chan continued this trend with martial arts action films containing even more elaborate and dangerous stunts, including <i><a href="/wiki/Wheels_on_Meals" title="Wheels on Meals">Wheels on Meals</a></i> (1984), <i><a href="/wiki/Police_Story_(1985_film)" title="Police Story (1985 film)">Police Story</a></i> (1985), <i><a href="/wiki/Armour_of_God_(film)" title="Armour of God (film)">Armour of God</a></i> (1986), <i><a href="/wiki/Project_A_Part_II" title="Project A Part II">Project A Part II</a></i> (1987), <i><a href="/wiki/Police_Story_2" title="Police Story 2">Police Story 2</a></i> (1988), and <i><a href="/wiki/Dragons_Forever" title="Dragons Forever">Dragons Forever</a></i> (1988). Other new trends which began in the 1980s were the "<a href="/wiki/Girls_with_guns" title="Girls with guns">girls with guns</a>" subgenre, for which <a href="/wiki/Michelle_Yeoh" title="Michelle Yeoh">Michelle Yeoh</a> gained fame; and especially the "<a href="/wiki/Heroic_bloodshed" title="Heroic bloodshed">heroic bloodshed</a>" genre, revolving around <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Triad_(underground_societies)" title="Triad (underground societies)">Triads</a>, largely pioneered by <a href="/wiki/John_Woo" title="John Woo">John Woo</a> and for which <a href="/wiki/Chow_Yun-fat" title="Chow Yun-fat">Chow Yun-fat</a> became famous. These Hong Kong action trends were later adopted by many Hollywood action films in the 1990s and 2000s.
</p><p>In Indian cinema, another star actor, considered by many to be the most natural actor in Indian cinema, <a href="/wiki/Mohanlal" title="Mohanlal">Mohanlal</a> acted in his first movie. During the 80's he rose to become a super star in Indian cinema. Indian cinema as a whole was changing in a new wave of movies and directors.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="1990s">1990s</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_film&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: 1990s">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/1990s_in_film" title="1990s in film">1990s in film</a></div>
<p>The early 1990s saw the development of a commercially successful independent cinema in the United States. Although cinema was increasingly dominated by special-effects films such as <i><a href="/wiki/Terminator_2:_Judgment_Day" title="Terminator 2: Judgment Day">Terminator 2: Judgment Day</a></i> (1991), <i><a href="/wiki/Jurassic_Park_(film)" title="Jurassic Park (film)">Jurassic Park</a></i> (1993) and <i><a href="/wiki/Titanic_(1997_film)" title="Titanic (1997 film)">Titanic</a></i> (1997), the latter of which became the highest-grossing film of all time at the time up until <i><a href="/wiki/Avatar_(2009_film)" title="Avatar (2009 film)">Avatar</a></i> (2009), also directed by James Cameron, independent films like <a href="/wiki/Steven_Soderbergh" title="Steven Soderbergh">Steven Soderbergh</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Sex,_Lies,_and_Videotape" title="Sex, Lies, and Videotape">Sex, Lies, and Videotape</a></i> (1989) and <a href="/wiki/Quentin_Tarantino" title="Quentin Tarantino">Quentin Tarantino</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Reservoir_Dogs" title="Reservoir Dogs">Reservoir Dogs</a></i> (1992) had significant commercial success both at the cinema and on home video.
</p><p>Filmmakers associated with the Danish film movement <a href="/wiki/Dogme_95" title="Dogme 95">Dogme 95</a> introduced a manifesto aimed to purify filmmaking. Its first few films gained worldwide critical acclaim, after which the movement slowly faded out.
</p><p>Scorsese's <a href="/wiki/Goodfellas" title="Goodfellas">Goodfellas</a> was released in 1990. It is considered by many as one of the greatest movies to be made, particularly in the gangster genre. It is said to be the highest point of Scorsese's career.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:1995cinema_admissions.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="625" data-file-width="1425" decoding="async" height="96" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/1995cinema_admissions.png/220px-1995cinema_admissions.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/1995cinema_admissions.png/330px-1995cinema_admissions.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/1995cinema_admissions.png/440px-1995cinema_admissions.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:1995cinema_admissions.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Cinema admissions in 1995</div></div></div>
<p>Major American studios began to create their own <a href="/wiki/Independent_film" title="Independent film">"independent" production companies</a> to finance and produce non-mainstream fare. One of the most successful independents of the 1990s, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Miramax_Films" title="Miramax Films">Miramax Films</a>, was bought by Disney the year before the release of Tarantino's runaway hit <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pulp_Fiction_(film)" title="Pulp Fiction (film)">Pulp Fiction</a></i> in 1994. The same year marked the beginning of film and video distribution online. Animated films aimed at family audiences also regained their popularity, with Disney's <i><a href="/wiki/Beauty_and_the_Beast_(1991_film)" title="Beauty and the Beast (1991 film)">Beauty and the Beast</a></i> (1991), <i><a href="/wiki/Aladdin_(1992_Disney_film)" title="Aladdin (1992 Disney film)">Aladdin</a></i> (1992), and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Lion_King" title="The Lion King">The Lion King</a></i> (1994). During 1995, the first feature-length <a href="/wiki/Computer_animation" title="Computer animation">computer-animated</a> feature, <i><a href="/wiki/Toy_Story" title="Toy Story">Toy Story</a></i>, was produced by <a href="/wiki/Pixar" title="Pixar">Pixar Animation Studios</a> and released by Disney. After the success of Toy Story, computer animation would grow to become the dominant technique for feature-length animation, which would allow competing film companies such as <a href="/wiki/DreamWorks_Pictures" title="DreamWorks Pictures">DreamWorks</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/20th_Century_Fox" title="20th Century Fox">20th Century Fox</a> and <a href="/wiki/Warner_Bros." title="Warner Bros.">Warner Bros.</a> to effectively compete with Disney with successful films of their own. During the late 1990s, another cinematic transition began, from physical film stock to <a href="/wiki/Digital_cinema" title="Digital cinema">digital cinema</a> technology. Meanwhile, <a href="/wiki/DVD" title="DVD">DVDs</a> became the new standard for consumer video, replacing VHS tapes.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="2000s">2000s</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_film&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: 2000s">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/2000s_in_film" title="2000s in film">2000s in film</a></div>
<p>Since the late 2000s <a href="/wiki/Streaming_media" title="Streaming media">streaming media</a> platforms like <a href="/wiki/YouTube" title="YouTube">YouTube</a> provided means for anyone with access to internet and cameras (a standard feature of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Smartphones" title="Smartphones">smartphones</a>) to publish videos to the world. Also competing with the increasing popularity of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Video_games" title="Video games">video games</a> and other forms of <a class="mw-redirect mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Home_entertainment_(disambiguation)" title="Home entertainment (disambiguation)">home entertainment</a>, the industry once again started to make theatrical releases more attractive, with new 3D technologies and epic (fantasy and superhero) films becoming a mainstay in cinemas.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Documentary_film" title="Documentary film">documentary film</a> also rose as a commercial genre for perhaps the first time, with the success of films such as <i><a href="/wiki/March_of_the_Penguins" title="March of the Penguins">March of the Penguins</a></i> and <a href="/wiki/Michael_Moore" title="Michael Moore">Michael Moore</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Bowling_for_Columbine" title="Bowling for Columbine">Bowling for Columbine</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Fahrenheit_9/11" title="Fahrenheit 9/11">Fahrenheit 9/11</a></i>. A new genre was created with <a href="/wiki/Martin_Kunert" title="Martin Kunert">Martin Kunert</a> and <a href="/wiki/Eric_Manes" title="Eric Manes">Eric Manes</a>' <i><a href="/wiki/Voices_of_Iraq" title="Voices of Iraq">Voices of Iraq</a></i>, when 150 inexpensive DV cameras were distributed across Iraq, transforming ordinary people into collaborative filmmakers. The success of <i><a href="/wiki/Gladiator_(2000_film)" title="Gladiator (2000 film)">Gladiator</a></i> led to a revival of interest in <a href="/wiki/Epic_film" title="Epic film">epic cinema</a>, and <i><a href="/wiki/Moulin_Rouge!" title="Moulin Rouge!">Moulin Rouge!</a></i> renewed interest in <a href="/wiki/Musical_film" title="Musical film">musical cinema</a>. <a href="/wiki/Home_cinema" title="Home cinema">Home theatre</a> systems became increasingly sophisticated, as did some of the special edition <a href="/wiki/DVD" title="DVD">DVDs</a> designed to be shown on them. <i><a href="/wiki/The_Lord_of_the_Rings_(film_series)" title="The Lord of the Rings (film series)">The Lord of the Rings trilogy</a></i> was released on DVD in both the theatrical version and in a special extended version intended only for home cinema audiences.
</p><p>In 2001, the <a href="/wiki/Harry_Potter_(film_series)" title="Harry Potter (film series)"><i>Harry Potter</i> film series</a> began, and by its end in 2011, it had become the highest-grossing film franchise of all time until the Marvel Cinematic Universe passed it in 2015.
</p><p>More films were also being released simultaneously to <a href="/wiki/IMAX" title="IMAX">IMAX</a> cinema, the first was in 2002's Disney animation <i><a href="/wiki/Treasure_Planet" title="Treasure Planet">Treasure Planet</a></i>; and the first live action was in 2003's <i><a href="/wiki/The_Matrix_Revolutions" title="The Matrix Revolutions">The Matrix Revolutions</a></i> and a re-release of <i><a href="/wiki/The_Matrix_Reloaded" title="The Matrix Reloaded">The Matrix Reloaded</a></i>. Later in the decade, <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Dark_Knight_(film)" title="The Dark Knight (film)">The Dark Knight</a></i> was the first major feature film to have been at least partially shot in IMAX technology.
</p><p>There has been an increasing globalization of cinema during this decade, with foreign-language films gaining popularity in English-speaking markets. Examples of such films include <i><a href="/wiki/Crouching_Tiger,_Hidden_Dragon" title="Crouching Tiger, Hidden Dragon">Crouching Tiger, Hidden Dragon</a></i> (Mandarin), <i><a href="/wiki/Am%C3%A9lie" title="Amélie">Amélie</a></i> (French), <i><a href="/wiki/Lagaan" title="Lagaan">Lagaan</a></i> (Hindi), <i><a href="/wiki/Spirited_Away" title="Spirited Away">Spirited Away</a></i> (Japanese), <i><a href="/wiki/City_of_God_(2002_film)" title="City of God (2002 film)">City of God</a></i> (Brazilian Portuguese), <i><a href="/wiki/The_Passion_of_the_Christ" title="The Passion of the Christ">The Passion of the Christ</a></i> (Aramaic), <i><a href="/wiki/Apocalypto" title="Apocalypto">Apocalypto</a></i> (Mayan) and <i><a href="/wiki/Inglourious_Basterds" title="Inglourious Basterds">Inglourious Basterds</a></i> (multiple European languages). Italy is the most awarded country at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Academy_Award_for_Best_Foreign_Language_Film" title="Academy Award for Best Foreign Language Film">Academy Award for Best Foreign Language Film</a>, with 14 awards won, 3 Special Awards and 31 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_Italian_submissions_for_the_Academy_Award_for_Best_Foreign_Language_Film" title="List of Italian submissions for the Academy Award for Best Foreign Language Film">nominations</a>.
</p><p>In 2003, there was a revival in 3D film popularity the first being James Cameron's <i><a href="/wiki/Ghosts_of_the_Abyss" title="Ghosts of the Abyss">Ghosts of the Abyss</a></i> which was released as the first full-length 3-D IMAX feature filmed with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reality_Camera_System" title="Reality Camera System">Reality Camera System</a>. This camera system used the latest HD video cameras, not film, and was built for Cameron by Emmy nominated Director of Photography Vince Pace, to his specifications. The same camera system was used to film <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spy_Kids_3D:_Game_Over" title="Spy Kids 3D: Game Over">Spy Kids 3D: Game Over</a></i> (2003), <i><a href="/wiki/Aliens_of_the_Deep" title="Aliens of the Deep">Aliens of the Deep</a></i> IMAX (2005), and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Adventures_of_Sharkboy_and_Lavagirl_in_3-D" title="The Adventures of Sharkboy and Lavagirl in 3-D">The Adventures of Sharkboy and Lavagirl in 3-D</a></i> (2005).
</p><p>After James Cameron's 3D film <i><a href="/wiki/Avatar_(2009_film)" title="Avatar (2009 film)">Avatar</a></i> became the highest-grossing film of all time, 3D films gained brief popularity with many other films being released in 3D, with the best critical and financial successes being in the field of feature film animation such as <a href="/wiki/Universal_Pictures" title="Universal Pictures">Universal Pictures</a>/<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Illumination_Entertainment" title="Illumination Entertainment">Illumination Entertainment</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Despicable_Me" title="Despicable Me">Despicable Me</a></i> and <a href="/wiki/DreamWorks_Animation" title="DreamWorks Animation">DreamWorks Animation</a>'s <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/How_To_Train_Your_Dragon_(film)" title="How To Train Your Dragon (film)">How To Train Your Dragon</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Shrek_Forever_After" title="Shrek Forever After">Shrek Forever After</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Megamind" title="Megamind">Megamind</a></i>. <i>Avatar</i> is also note-worthy for pioneering highly sophisticated use of motion capture technology and influencing several other films such as <i><a href="/wiki/Rise_of_the_Planet_of_the_Apes" title="Rise of the Planet of the Apes">Rise of the Planet of the Apes</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="2010s">2010s</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_film&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: 2010s">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/2010s_in_film" title="2010s in film">2010s in film</a></div>
<p>As of 2011<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_film&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, the largest film industries by number of feature films produced were those of India, the United States, China, Nigeria and Japan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup>
</p><p>In Hollywood, <a href="/wiki/Superhero_film" title="Superhero film">superhero films</a> have greatly increased in popularity and financial success, with films based on <a href="/wiki/Marvel_Comics" title="Marvel Comics">Marvel</a> and <a href="/wiki/DC_Comics" title="DC Comics">DC comics</a> regularly being released every year up to the present.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup> As of 2019<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_film&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, the superhero genre has been the most dominant genre as far as <a href="/wiki/List_of_highest-grossing_films_in_the_United_States_and_Canada" title="List of highest-grossing films in the United States and Canada">American box office receipts</a> are concerned. The 2019 superhero film <i><a href="/wiki/Avengers:_Endgame" title="Avengers: Endgame">Avengers: Endgame</a></i>, was the most successful movie of all-time at the box office.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="2020s">2020s</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_film&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: 2020s">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/2020s_in_film" title="2020s in film">2020s in film</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="COVID-19_pandemic">COVID-19 pandemic</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_film&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: COVID-19 pandemic">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Impact_of_the_COVID-19_pandemic_on_cinema" title="Impact of the COVID-19 pandemic on cinema">Impact of the COVID-19 pandemic on cinema</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/COVID-19_pandemic" title="COVID-19 pandemic">COVID-19 pandemic</a> resulted in closures of film theatres around the world in response to regional and national lockdowns. Many films slated to release in the early 2020s faced delays in development, production, and distribution, with others being released on streaming services with little or no theatrical window.
</p>
